The 22 completed work authorizations involving survey and impact evaluations for the Atlanta,
Dallas, Lufkin, Paris, and Tyler districts (Table 2) consisted of seven in the Atlanta District
(composed of Bowie, Camp, Cass, Harrison, Marion, Morris, Panola, Titus, and Upshur
counties), three in the Dallas District (composed of Collin, Dallas, Denton, Ellis, Kaufman,
Navarro, and Rockwall counties), nine in the Lufkin District (composed of Angelina, Houston,
Nacogdoches, Polk, Sabine, San Augustine, San Jacinto, Shelby, and Trinity counties), two in the
Paris District (composed of Delta, Fannin, Franklin, Grayson, Hopkins, Hunt, Lamar, Rains, and
Red River counties), and one in the Tyler District (composed of Anderson, Cherokee, Gregg,
Henderson, Rusk, Smith, Van Zandt, and Wood counties). These work authorizations comprised
the following projects:
• Atlanta District: seven archeological surveys involving one bridge replacement, one bridge
replacement/road-widening, one bank stabilization, one road widening, two safety treatments,
and one transportation enhancement project.
• Dallas District: two surveys and one impact evaluation involving one airport extension and
two new construction projects.
• Lufkin District: seven archeological surveys and two impact evaluations involving one
bridge replacement, two bridge replacements with road widening/realignment, one new
construction, two road widenings, and three safety treatments within an existing ROW.

IMPACTS AND SITE POTENTIAL
One of the principal concerns of both impact assessments and surveys is the identification of
archeological historic properties (36 CFR 800.16.[1]) and State Archeological Landmarks (SALs;
13 TAC 26.12). Disturbances, whether natural or man-made (Table 3), diminish the possibility of
finding such properties. In the impact evaluation phase, evaluating disturbances can assist in
identifying areas where further survey is not necessary. Conversely, identifying areas possessing
a high potential for containing archeological historic properties or SALs is facilitated by an
awareness of the effects of disturbance. Whether classified as man-made fill, man-made cut, or
naturally occurring, certain disturbances occur in existing ROWs with such frequency that it is
possible to form a few generalized observations regarding their extent and effect on the
landscape.
Cut-and-fill episodes occur when a road or bridge approach is elevated above the flood plain. Fill
for the road berm is often obtained from the margins within the ROW and deposited in the center
of the ROW. The result is a raised road berm bordered by depressed areas, which function to
facilitate drainage runoff. If the slope at the junction of the terrace above the flood plain with the
road or bridge is steep or sudden, a road cut is sometimes utilized to lessen the incline without
raising the road higher. For roads situated parallel with the slope, a road cut on the high side of
the slope is sometimes necessary for a level road. Fill from the road cut may be deposited on the
low side, or used elsewhere. At the immediate junction of the road or bridge with the terrace, a
variety of methods can be employed to hold the shoulders in place against erosion. These can
include concrete or stone aprons or less formally designed sheet metal or wooden aprons.
Road berms are generally 1–3 m in height on narrow rural roads, but can be up to 5–6 m in height
on major highways or over wide flood plains. The extent of the raised berm shoulders can be
quite wide, ranging from 10 m, the limits of the ROW on narrow, one-lane, rural roads, to 20–23
m on larger roads. Borrow areas or drainage ditches are approximately 0.5–1.0 m deep on
smaller roads, but can extend up to 1.5 m. Both trenching and shovel testing reveal that
disturbances from earth-working equipment can extend 0.5 m and deeper.
Underground utilities are typically placed outside the limits of road berms, near the margins of
the ROW, and can be stacked vertically, or spaced horizontally. Telephone, fiber optic, and
electrical lines can be laid shallowly in narrow trenches of approximately 1 m in width. Fiber
optic cables are laid farthest from the road, usually at the outer edge of the ROW and outside of
older phone and electric cables. In addition, gas and water lines exposed at stream crossings have
been observed at depths of over 4 m. Sewer and fire hydrant as well as gas and water line
placement is a consideration in residential and industrial areas, though these are not typically

marked. Aboveground utility transmission lines are typically placed toward the margins of the
ROW. Within any given ROW, it is possible that 1–3 m of horizontal utility installation
disturbances exist.
Because of their association with flood plains, many of these project areas would naturally
undergo depositional processes due to periodic alluviation. Man-made disturbances, however,
exacerbate the natural effects of erosion by altering drainage patterns. Bridge aprons, road berms,
and drainage ditches channel rain runoff through the ROW, causing intense erosion of surface
soils, cutbanks, and terrace edges adjacent to these features. The barbed-wire fence, a common
feature in rural areas, traps debris in its mesh, which hinders surface water from draining into
contiguous areas. Another trap along creeks is the wire or slat and wire “fence” strung across a
creek to trap large debris at bridges and culverts. The backup of large debris tends to cause
extensive erosion at the edges of bridges or culverts compromising the structure as well as the
road crossing it. Utility trenches are typically backfilled with the original matrix, and these
loosened soils erode quickly in areas of heightened runoff.
Other disturbances, which have been observed in existing ROWs as well as the adjacent areas and
therefore have the potential to be located in new ROWs, are usually associated with residential,
commercial, or rural development. In residential areas, improvements can include landscaping,
improved drainages, culvert driveways, curb cuts, and construction. Commercial improvements
that have been encountered in project areas have been railroad beds, dams and their associated
reservoirs, berms, clear cutting, and trash dumping. In rural areas, evidence of plowing, logging,
ranching, and stock pond construction is apparent. Also note that the first stage of
highway/bridge construction consists of vegetation removal by bulldozer in order to remove both
trees and roots.

CHAPTER 5
SITE INVESTIGATIONS

Twenty-eight archeological sites or plotted site locations were investigated during the execution
of this contract. Because the scope of work for testing 41DL203 was prepared under this
contract, this site has been included although no fieldwork was conducted; testing was initiated
under a separate contract.. Descriptions of these sites and recommendations for treatment are
drawn from the original work authorization reports and are summarized in Table 4.

SITE 41AG180 (WA 26)

Previous Investigations
Site 41AG180, was recorded within the current APE, by Horizon ESI, on a sandy knoll on the
west bank of Peach Creek, adjacent to Kit McConnico Park during the survey of a 17-acre annex
for the McConnico Park commissioned by the City of Lufkin in 2002 (Brownlow 2002). Site
41AG180 was reported to be delineated by Peach Creek to the east, a small drainage and
transmission line to the north, a fence line and railroad tracks to the south, and Loop 287 to the
west. The Texas Archeological Sites Atlas depiction of the site location shows the apparent
location of the shovel tests adjacent to the top bank of Peach Creek, about 100 m from the edge of
Loop 287, but with a much larger assumed site area drawn along the 260-ft contour line that
extends to Loop 287. The material recovered from three of four shovel tests at 41AG180 in 2002
included silicified wood debitage, sandy paste ceramics, bone, and charcoal, below historic glass,
metal, and whiteware. Cultural material extended at least 1 m in depth. The site was assessed as
having potential for both NRHP listing and SAL designation.

Current Investigations
Geo-Marine, Inc., archeologists conducted fieldwork on September 4 and 5, 2007, within the new
proposed ROW in an attempt to document previously recorded site 41AG180. The site is located
in a cleared pasture owned by Angelina & Neches River Railroad and bounded to the west by
Loop 287 North, to the south by the railroad tracks, to the north by a transmission line, and to the
east by Peach Creek. The geology of the area is Eocene Yegua Formation and the mapped soils

are Alazan very fine sandy loam, 0 to 4% slopes, and Alazan-Urban land complex, 0 to 4% slopes
(Dolezel 1988). The profile from Shovel Test 1, in approximately the middle of the original
mapped site boundary and within the current APE, consisted of 0–19 cm of dark yellowish brown
(10YR 4/4 dry) silty clay loam with organic material, over 19–60 cm bs of dark yellowish brown
(10YR 4/6 dry) sandy loam, over 60–70 cm bs of light brownish gray (10YR 6/2 dry) silty clay
with abundant redoximorphic iron features. Within the existing ROW, the primary vegetation is
regularly maintained (mown) grass and associated forbs. The proposed ROW consists mainly of
recently clear-cut pine forest with secondary vegetation of tall grasses and weeds.

69

GMI archeologists excavated five mechanical trenches and four 50-x-50-cm shovel tests within
the extended site area within the proposed highway ROW. All of the shovel tests and trenches
were excavated within the privately owned pasture. No cultural material was recovered from any
of the trenches or shovel tests; however, 27 pieces of petrified wood, 32 small rounded gravel,
and 13 pieces of ironstone pebbles were recovered during these investigations. None of this
material exhibited signs of cultural modification. The originally reported boundary of site
41AG180 appeared to be incorrect, as it was based on only three shovel tests and then expanded
to include an entire landform with no supporting data and insufficient site delineation. As a result
of these investigations, it was determined that no portion of site 41AG180 extended into the
existing or proposed ROW. No additional investigations within the APE were recommended.

SITE 41AG194 (WA 20)

Current Investigations
Site 41AG194 is a newly recorded site demonstrating both historic and prehistoric components
and was identified in Parcel 1 approximately 100 m northeast of the Neches River on a low-lying
terrace. The primary feature of the historic component is a rectangular wooden building that
appears to date to the early half of the twentieth century. It occurs approximately 45 m south of
the existing SH 94 ROW and about 20 m south (outside) of the proposed project APE.
The underlying geology of the site consists of Holocene alluvial deposits (BEG 1993). The soils
consist of Ozias-Pophers complex, 0 to 1 percent slopes, frequently flooded (Dolezel 1988). Soil
profiles were generally consistent and consisted of 5–40 cm of brown (10YR 4/3) fine sandy
loam above a strong brown (7.5YR 5/6) fine sandy loam that extended to the bottom of the
units—usually 80 cm bs. Vegetation consists of regularly maintained (mown) grasses, wetland
vegetation, pine forest interspersed with oak and sweet gum trees and shrubs.
The wooden building was constructed with wire nails, has a corrugated metal roof, and measures
roughly 18 m east to west. The east half is approximately 4 m wide north to south and contains
an electric stove and a few pieces of furniture. A fuse box dangles from the north side of this
portion. Two strips of corrugated metal are nailed to the west end of the building, possibly
covering old windows. The west half is about a meter wider and is partially collapsed under a
fallen tree. An abandoned refrigerator is visible in this area, but this segment of the house
appears to have functioned primarily for storage purposes based on the windows, which are
covered with screen but lack glass panes unlike the east half of the structure.
Although the structure was examined, subsurface investigations were restricted to the APE north
of the structure. Twenty-two shovel tests were excavated in Parcel 1, private property east of the
Neches River, and two additional shovel tests were placed in the existing ROW directly north of
the property (Figure 11). Shovel tests within the proposed ROW typically consisted of a brown
or dark grayish brown fine sandy loam surface layer that graded into dark yellowish brown fine
sandy loam and eventually to strong brown clayey sand. Two shovel tests were excavated in the
existing ROW and revealed a much different profile: ST 23 and ST 24 (both with modern bottle
glass, asphalt, and gravel; 0–60 cm bs) demonstrated a much more complex, stratified profile that
likely represents different layers of fill deposited when the highway was constructed.

70

Historic artifacts possibly associated with occupation of the building were identified in several
shovel tests: a piece of blue-tinted white whiteware (1880–1930); a 17-cm-long piece of rebar
and pieces of metal similar to the corrugated metal on the structure; several fragments of clear
and brown machine-made bottle glass (post 1910), four wire nails (post-1890), including a 6-inch
spike; window glass; a .22-caliber shell (post-1859); a crown bottle cap (post-1905); and several
asphalt pieces. Surficial material consisted of a metal rod of unknown function approximately 3
ft in length; refuse of glass bottles, metal cans, and plastic bottles around the structure and within
the APE (dating from the early or middle 1900s to the present); and the remnants of a cast-iron
highway torch or smudge pot (used by highway departments between the 1920s and 1970s as a
warning signal to motorists [Vandercook 2007] and thus more likely related to construction of SH
94 than to activities associated with the structure).
The prehistoric component of the site was clustered in the northcentral part of the site.
Subsurface material consisted of four pieces of pottery, several pieces of silicified wood shatter,
and chert and quartzite flakes, all stratigraphically below the historic material.
The historic component of 41AG194 was determined to be fairly recent and multiple occupations
and/or dumping episodes had compromised the contextual integrity of the site. The building was
outside the APE and would not be impacted by the proposed project. The prehistoric component
within the APE appeared sparse; however, the materials identified tended to occur in relatively
deep contexts. Although several shovel tests were excavated as deeply as possible (> 100 cm bs)
in this area (backhoe trenches not allowed) and the prehistoric materials appeared to be primarily
circumscribed between 60–80 cm bs, more deeply buried archeological materials could be present
because of the alluvial setting. Therefore, additional investigation was recommended once the
state obtained Parcel 1 to determine whether the artifact deposit observed represented a lowdensity scatter or overlay a deeper, more significant component. Recent trenching by TxDOT,
after acquisition of the land parcel within the APE, confirmed the sparse nature of archeological
material within highly bioturbated soils. No additional work within the APE was recommended,
and THC concurred that the portion of the site within the proposed ROW was not eligible for
lising in the NRHP or for designation as an SAL.

SITE 41AN162 (WA 5)

Previous Investigations
A member of the Texas Archeological Stewardship Network originally recorded the site in 2003;
however, the site location is not plotted on the Texas Archeological Sites Atlas and despite two
separate conversations with the property owner, Mr. Archie Stivner, he never mentioned any
previous archeological investigations on the property. Rather, he stated that although he assumed
that archeological materials would be present on the property given its proximity to 41AN19, he
has never seen any artifacts (Archie Stivner, personal communications 26 and 28 July 2006).
Consequently, current investigators were unaware of the presence of a recorded site potentially
within the project APE. The original site form lists the site centroid coordinates as being farther
north and east of those derived from the present survey. However, the original investigator did
not shovel test the area to delineate the site’s boundaries, and the present survey was limited to
the project APE. Furthermore, the general location and types of artifacts observed in 2003 (a
subsurface petrified wood flake; and surface material of historic whiteware and colorless bottle
72

glass, and approximately 10 prehistoric sherds) are consistent with material recovered currently,
and TARL is considering the site reported under the present investigation to be an extension of
41AN19.

Current Investigations
The site is located on the north side of US 175 immediately west of a large, fairly level terrace,
where the landform begins to slope toward the Neches River, which is directly to the east. A
natural spring occurs south of the site on the south side of US 175, and 41AN19 lies
approximately 250 m to the northwest. The underlying geology is the Eocene Weches Formation
as well as Holocene alluvium, and the mapped soil is Elrose fine sandy loam, 1 to 3 percent
slopes (Coffee 1975). Shovel tests rarely extended deeper than 40 cm below surface (bs) and
consisted of reddish yellow (7.5YR 6/6) compact fine sandy loam above yellowish red (5YR 5/8)
compact sandy clay. The eastern fifth of the survey area demonstrated up to 50 cm of light brown
(7.5YR 6/4) fine sandy loam with hematite gravels above a similar subsoil. The vegetation
consists of grass with scattered mature pine and hardwoods; hardwoods are along the east and
southeast fence lines.
Cultural materials were identified in seven shovel tests immediately west of the beginning of the
slope toward the river (Figure 12). Artifacts consisted of five pieces of plain prehistoric pottery,
one fragment of incised prehistoric pottery, one petrified wood flake, five chert flakes, one piece
of historic glass, and two historic ceramic fragments. Artifact density was generally low in the
portion of the site that falls within project APE, and positive units typically only produced a
single flake or piece of pottery. No artifacts were collected in accordance with the wishes of the
property owner.
Despite the fact that site 41AN162 has some contextual integrity inconsistencies, it is located near
the east end of the top of a large terrace overlooking the Neches River to the east, and it is
approximately 250–300 m southeast of 41AN19, an SAL-designated and NRHP-listed Caddo
site. A natural spring also occurs south of the site on the opposite side of US 175. Therefore, the
potential for subsurface features exists, and further investigation was recommended.

SITE 41AN173 (WA 5)

Current Investigations
The site occurs on the north side of US 175, approximately 800 m north of Caddo Creek on a
gentle, south-facing slope of a terrace that overlooks the stream. It is presently a vacant,
overgrown field with grasses, blackberry and other brush, hardwoods along fence lines and a
stand at the northern boundary on the east end, and a pile of modern asphalt shingles dumped in
the southwest corner. The underlying geology of the area is the Eocene Queen City Sand
formation. The mapped soil is Elrose fine sandy loam, 3 to 8 percent slopes (Coffee 1975),
although the soils in the shovel tests typically consisted of 15 to 60 cm of reddish yellow (7.5YR
6/6) fine sandy loam, often containing hematite gravels, above strong brown (7.5 YR 5/8)
compact sandy clay loam.

73

Initially, two transects were shovel-tested, and three units each produced one to two tertiary chert
flakes in the upper 30 cm (Figure 13). Due to the distribution of the positive shovel tests (two at
the southeast corner and one in the northwest end), a third transect was excavated to the north.
Although this transect was later determined to be outside the APE, two pieces of prehistoric
pottery nevertheless were identified along this transect in the easternmost shovel test at 30 cm bs.
Only one artifact was encountered in subsequent delineation shovel tests excavated around the
positive units. A shovel test, limited to 5 m south of Transect A, ST 2, by the property fence,
yielded a single chert flake in the upper 20 cm. No artifacts were collected in accordance with
the wishes of the property owner.
The three positive shovel tests near the southeast corner of the survey area are clustered within a
radius of approximately 25 m and have been designated as site 41AN173; the two more remote
units are considered isolated finds. It is likely that the site once extended beyond its present
limits but has largely been destroyed by plowing, the construction of US 175 to the immediate
south, and residential structures on the south side of the highway.
The majority of site 41AN173 probably existed in this vicinity but has since been heavily
disturbed. The low artifact density and the compromised contextual integrity from the impacts of
plowing and construction resulted in very limited research potential, and thus Geo-Marine
recommended no additional work. within the current APE. However, because of the potential for
subsurface features, TxDOT has proposed additional investigation (scraping) once the land parcel
has been acquired.

SITE 41CE404 (WA 5)

Current Investigations
The site occurs midway along the elevation of a ridge north of US 175 and about 850 meters west
of the west branch of Gum Creek. A natural spring is located approximately 700 m to the west,
on a neighboring property. Gas and petroleum pipelines cross the survey area approximately 20
m southeast of the site boundary, an overhead utility crosses the site northeast–southwest, and a
partially paved driveway borders the western site of the site. The underlying geology of the area
is the Eocene Queen City Sand. The mapped soils are Cuthbert and Ruston fine sandy loam,
strongly sloping (30 percent); Eustis loamy fine sand, strongly sloping (30 percent); Ruston and
Bowie loamy fine sands, strongly sloping (10 percent); Nacogdoches fine sandy loam, sloping
(10 percent) (Mowery 1959). Typical soils in the shovel tests in the east half of the survey area,
however, characteristically consisted of pink (7.5YR 7/3–8/3) gravelly fine sandy loam that
progressively became more compact with depth but extended beyond 80 cm bs. Soils on the west
side were generally composed of 40 to 60 cm of light yellowish brown (10YR 6/4) gravelly fine
sand above reddish brown (5YR 4/6) compact sand. Vegetation consists of grasses and pine
seedlings flanked by a mixed pine and hardwood forest with thick underbrush.
Site 41CE404 consists of a 540-square-meter, low-density concentration of tertiary stone flakes.
The APE was investigated in two transects starting from the east end (Figure 14). Initial shovel
test intervals were set at 50 m because of the sloping terrain, thick vegetation, and deadfall. A
low-density lithic deposit (site 41CE404) was identified approximately midway along the survey
area, and once the site was delineated and transect shovel testing resumed, intervals were reduced
75

to 30 m. Site 41CE404 was first encountered when a chert flake was recovered from ST 2 of
Transect B at approximately 40 cm bs in a clearing of grass and pine seedlings between two
densely wooded areas. Another flake was identified 60 cm bs in a shovel test 10 m east of the
first positive unit, and three were recovered at 50 cm bs 10 m south of ST B2. A shovel test 10 m
west of the first positive unit produced seven flakes between 20 and 60 cm bs. The site was
originally thought to extend an additional 15 m north with a possible flake recovered between 0
and 20 cm bs from a shovel test west of the driveway inside a forested area. However, the
property owner permitted collection of artifacts and later examination of the object indicated that
it was not a flake. Additional delineation shovel tests were sterile, and the overall area
demonstrates disturbances. Overhead power lines extend roughly north-south across the site, and
a two-track access road for the power line and pipeline easements that cross the survey area
extends east from the driveway across the site. A pushpile of dirt and rubble was also observed
southwest of a delineation shovel test 20 m south of ST B2.
The general site area has been cleared to access overhead and buried utility easements, and the
pushpile of debris is located about 15 m south of the site. A driveway for a residence also
extends along the west side of the site, although no artifacts were identified in shovel tests west of
the driveway. Site 41CE404 demonstrates limited research potential because of the surrounding
disturbances and sparseness of the artifact deposit. Consequently, no further work was
recommended within the APE.

SITE 41CE405 (WA 5)

Current Investigations
Site 41CE405 is in an area that extends along a prominent terrace on the north side of US 175,
approximately 500 m north of a tributary of Jordan Creek. The majority of the site occurs along
the south slope of the terrace, although a small portion extends along the upper part of the
landform as well. The underlying geology of the area is the Eocene Sparta Sand and Weches
Formation. The mapped soils consist of Bub-Nacogdoches complex (52 percent); Bowie loamy
fine sand, gently sloping (38 percent); and Lakeland loamy fine sand, nearly level (10 percent)
(Mowery 1959). The soils in the shovel tests generally consisted of 10 to 30 cm of light
yellowish brown (10YR 6/4) gravelly fine sandy loam above 10 to 20 cm of brownish yellow
(10YR 6/6) extremely gravelly and compact fine sandy loam. Subsoil was a strong brown
(7.5YR 5/8) clayey sandy loam. The vegetation consists of grasses and an immature hardwood
forest containing a few mature cedar and live oak trees.
The area was traversed in four 20-m transects due to landowner reports of possible Caddo
mounds or burial sites on the property. Initially, shovel tests were spaced at 20-m intervals;
however, the level of effort was reduced to surface inspection after several units indicated
disturbance. Portions of Transects C and D extended across the top of the landform at the eastern
half of the APE where intact soils were present, and shovel testing resumed at 30-m intervals. No
artifacts were identified in these shovel tests (Figure 15). However, site 41CE405 was discovered
on the basis of three above-ground features. Feature 1 is on the top of the landform south of a
barbed wire fence and is hidden in a cluster of saplings. The feature, the remnants of a historic
building, is a partially collapsed, sandstone and mortar, square structure, approximately 2-x-2 m
with a corrugated metal roof and wooden roof supports held together with wire nails. Transect D
78

extended approximately 10 m south of the structure, but no artifacts were identified in the shovel
tests. However, one shovel test each was excavated 5 m east, west, and south of the structure. A
.22 caliber rimfire cartridge was recovered from the upper 20 cm of the western shovel test. No
additional shovel tests were excavated due to the paucity of artifacts and because it was suspected
that the structure fell outside the project APE, which later proved to be true. Feature 2, a midden
of glass tumblers, bottles, snuff bottles, canning jars, and medicine bottles, is about 65 m
southeast of Feature 1 as the landform begins to slope southward and is immediately within the
north tree line of the forested area that extends across the south face of the APE. Feature 3 is an
intact brick well, approximately 1 m in diameter, also on the south slope of the ridge, roughly 75
m southwest of the stone structure. The exterior is plastered with mortar, and the top has been
covered with metal. Metal cans, metal buckets, and glass bottles are scattered along the south
slope around Features 2 and 3.
A residential structure occurs about 150 m west of the stone structure, but its style does not
appear to predate the 1950s, and it is likely not associated with the well or building. Intact soils
are only present along the top of the ridge within the survey area, and despite a transect of shovel
tests roughly 10 m south of the structure, only one subsurface artifact was recovered. The
structure occurs north of the limits of the project APE, and investigations north of the structure
were not possible. The sides of the ridge consist of degrading ironstone. Consequently, all
cultural materials in this area were on the ground surface. Artifacts included a concentration of
glass vessels that likely date no earlier than 1925, as well as metal cans, metal buckets, and glass
bottles scattered along the south slope. Modern trash, consisting of guttering, window screen
mesh, and a vinyl awning or tablecloth, is intermixed with the scatter. The recent date of the
features and artifacts, the lack of contextual integrity due to repeated dumping episodes, and the
absence of intact, buried cultural deposits indicate a limited research potential for the site, and
thus no further archeological work within the APE was recommended. However, archival
investigation to determine the antiquity and possible residents of the house site at 41CE405 was
suggested.

SITE 41COL172 (WA 12)

Previous Investigations
Site 41COL172 is located on the southern bank of Muddy Creek and lies within the newly
proposed ROW associated with the widening and realignment of FM 1378 in Collin County. The
previous investigations conducted on this site initially included a combination of pedestrian
survey and shovel testing (Sills et al. 2005). The pedestrian survey and 26 shovel tests conducted
within the proposed realignment ROW for FM 1378 failed to identify any cultural remains or
reach subsoil. Therefore, it was decided that an adjusted strategy of excavating exploratory
archeological trenches would be undertaken to (1) evaluate the potential for in situ burial of
archeological deposits, and (2) search for buried archeological sites and evaluate the potential
effects of the proposed project on them. Two backhoe trenches were excavated on either side of
the existing Muddy Creek channel, as close to the creek as possible (Sills et al. 2005:6). Trenches
on the north side of the creek were culturally sterile, but faunal material consisting of deer and
possibly bison was found in the backdirt of both trenches on the southern bank of Muddy Creek.
The majority of the faunal material appeared to be located near the interface of the recent alluvial
deposits and the underlying buried soil, and some of the fragments appeared to possess “pot
80

polish” or the sheen often found on bones that have been boiled in ceramic vessels. Charcoal
flecks and pieces of mussel shell were also observed in the backdirt from these trenches. These
materials alone failed to provide convincing evidence of past cultural behavior, because they
could represent the byproduct of natural taphonomic processes. Nevertheless, given the potential
that the materials could be the result of cultural behavior, researchers recommended additional
work be conducted to assess fully the potential of the site (Sills et al. 2005:1–13).

Current Investigations
GMI returned to site 41COL172 on 18–22 November 2006 in an attempt to assess further the
research potential and eligibility of the site for inclusion in the NRHP and for designation as an
SAL. Site 41COL172 represents a discrete single-component Late Prehistoric site with
incredibly high rates of preservation. The presence of a single ceramic sherd attributable to the
type Pennington Punctated-Incised has provided a tentative date range of roughly A.D. 1000–
1200.
The underlying geology of the site is Holocene alluvium and the mapped soils are Trinity clay,
occasionally flooded (Hanson and Wheeler 1969), with a typical profile of 0 to 6 inches of very
dark gray (5Y 3/1) clay, dark gray (5Y 4/1) dry, moderate fine and medium granular and
moderate fine subangular blocky structure; above 6 to 16 inches of very dark gray (5Y 3/1) clay,
dark gray (5Y 4/1) dry, moderate medium subangular blocky structure; above 16 to 36 inches of
very dark gray (5Y 3/1) clay, dark gray (5Y 4/1) dry, weak fine and very fine subangular blocky
structure; above 36 to 64 inches of very dark gray (5Y 3/1) clay, dark gray (5Y 4/1) dry, weak
coarse blocky structure; common fine and medium distinct olive yellow (5Y 6/6) and yellowish
brown (10YR 5/8) redox concentrations; 64 to 75 inches; dark olive gray (5Y 3/2) clay, olive
gray (5Y 4/2) dry; weak coarse angular blocky structure; common fine and medium distinct olive
yellow (2.5Y 6/6; 5Y 6/8) and few coarse distinct light olive brown (2.5Y 5/4) redox
concentrations. The vegetation in the area is predominantly grassland with scattered clusters of
trees, with the greatest concentration of trees along the drainages.
GMI excavated six test trenches to further observe and document the geological setting of the
site. Once these profiles were investigated, GMI archeologists placed one 50-x-50-cm test
excavation unit off the edge of each trench to recover a representative sample of the cultural
material present in the cultural-bearing deposits. The cultural zone was encountered below the
modern alluvial deposits at an average depth of 1.3 m bs. The distinction between the overlying
modern alluvial deposits and the underlying cultural-bearing deposits was demarcated by an
easily identifiable unconformity. The cultural zone was roughly 70 cm thick. The depth of the
trenches was extended as a precautionary measure, ensuring the trenches extended deep enough
to fully expose the cultural-bearing layer.
Each trench position was documented using global positioning system (GPS) readings, and these
points were collected at both ends of the long axis of each trench and at the center point of the test
unit. Once the trenches were established and the overburden stripped from the location where the
test unit was to be located, GMI archeologists laid out 50-x-50-cm units with one side open to the
trench profile. These units were then excavated in 10-cm arbitrary levels with all excavated
materials screened through ¼-inch hardware cloth. These units were excavated to 10 cm below
the cultural-bearing layers or until it was obvious excavations had entered the AC horizon.

81

The horizontal extent of site 41COL172 is largely unknown because testing has been restricted to
the newly proposed ROW. The site is confirmed to be roughly 365 square meters, but assumed to
be larger. The northern portion of this site shares a northern boundary with the southern bank of
Muddy Creek. The remaining boundaries are hypothesized to be outlined by the paleochannel of
Muddy Creek. The eastern boundary of the site is associated with Trench E, and the western
boundary is assumed to extend beyond the present ROW. The southern boundary is also marked
by Trench E, because the evidence from Trench F is likely the result of natural processes rather
than cultural. Thus, in effect, the paleochannel of Muddy Creek marks the edge of an in situ
former terrace composed of West Fork soil. If this hypothesis is true, then the site extends to the
west and increases the aerial extent of site 41COL172 to 770 square meters.
The total number of artifacts recovered from the site is 541, with the vast majority of these
specimens being faunal material. The test units yielded 11 pieces of burned clay/earth, 175 bone
fragments, 87 shell fragments, 30 specimens of charcoal, 10 pieces of debitage, and 9 fire-cracked
rock (FCR). A total of 13 pieces of burned clay/earth was recovered during excavation. The 30
pieces of charcoal were collected from Test Unit 2, but no other unit produced charcoal. The
remainder of the material came from backhoe excavations.
A total of 379 bones was analyzed from this site. The most commonly identified faunal remains
from the site were 195 bones attributed to white-tailed deer. Eighteen bison bones were also
identified within the assemblage. The number of identifiable remains is greatly reduced in the
next tier of identifiable remains; there were two fragments of a rabbit-sized mammal, one fish
bone, one bone from a rat, one bone from a snake, and 21 fragments of multiple types of turtles.
Skeletal elements from three fish and three birds were thought to be part of the background static
associated with the natural setting of the site. The remaining specimens were unidentifiable
fragments.
The lithic assemblage from the site was composed of nine flakes, two pieces of shatter, two
pieces of red ochre, and 12 pieces of FCR. All lithic debitage specimens are byproducts from the
manufacture of stone tools from locally undifferentiated cherts commonly found within the
Uvalde gravels. Eight of these flakes are the result of bifacial thinning and can reasonably be
assumed to represent some aspect of either biface construction or maintenance. The other flake is
from a core or tested cobble. All of these flakes and the 12 fragments of FCR were recovered
from either Test Units 1 or 2, except for a piece of shatter recovered from Test Unit 5.
Site 41COL172 is a low-density, single-component site in an environment characterized by
rapidly aggrading sediments, which have contributed to heightened levels of preservation. The
site has been buried 1–1.3 m below the surface by rapidly accumulating alluvial deposits, has
exceptional levels of preservation allowing for the recovery of fauna material, and has intact
stratigraphic sequences containing diagnostic artifacts. Thus, the stratigraphic integrity is among
the best that could be hoped for. Therefore, GMI recommended that further work at site
41COL172 be undertaken, with a particular focus on that part of the site closest to the bank of
Muddy Creek in the area of the highest artifact densities. Additional eligibility testing was
subsequently undertaken at site 41COL172 under a later contract.

82

SITE 41COL173 (WA 12)

Previous Investigations
The site is located at the junction of FM 981 and Desert Creek, east of the town of Blue Ridge, in
Collin County. The previous investigations conducted on this site initially included a
combination of pedestrian survey and shovel testing (Sills et al. 2005b). The pedestrian survey
and nine shovel tests conducted within the extended ROW for FM 981 failed to identify any
cultural remains or reach subsoil. It was subsequently decided an adjusted strategy involving the
excavating of exploratory archeological trenches would be undertaken to (1) evaluate the
potential for in situ burial of archeological deposits, and (2) search for buried archeological sites
and evaluate the potential effects of the proposed project on them. The results of the adjusted
strategy included the excavation of three backhoe trenches placed as close to the creek banks as
possible. The trenches on the west bank produced culturally related materials, but the trench on
the east bank was culturally sterile (Sills et al. 2005b:4). The cultural material observed from the
trenches included faunal evidence field-identified as the remains of white-tailed deer and turtle,
and a medial fragment of a biface from a depth between 45–90 cm bs. PBS&J, the initial
researchers, recommended further investigation on the site in order to fully assess the research
potential prior to the beginning of construction (Sills et al. 2005b).

Current Investigations
Site 41COL173 is situated on a relatively flat, wooded terrace on the western bank of Desert
Creek. The landform is similar on both the north and south sides of FM 981. The northern side
of FM 981 has a series of partially filled channel scars, especially in the western portion of the
ROW. The southern side of FM 981 is completely flat, except for a deeply incised intermitted
streambed at the far western edge of the newly acquired ROW. The intermitted streambed runs
west-to-east until reaching the western edge of the ROW, and then turns sharply to the south
passing to the west of the southwestern ROW corner. A number of large bois d’arc trees are
scattered throughout the southern terrace.
The underlying geology of the site is Holocene alluvium, and the mapped soils are Trinity clay,
occasionally flooded (Hanson and Wheeler 1969), with a typical profile of 0 to 6 inches of very
dark gray (5Y 3/1) clay, dark gray (5Y 4/1) dry, moderate fine and medium granular and
moderate fine subangular blocky structure; over 6 to 16 inches of very dark gray (5Y 3/1) clay,
dark gray (5Y 4/1) dry, moderate medium subangular blocky structure; over 16 to 36 inches of
very dark gray (5Y 3/1) clay, dark gray (5Y 4/1) dry, weak fine and very fine subangular blocky
structure; over 36 to 64 inches of very dark gray (5Y 3/1) clay, dark gray (5Y 4/1) dry, weak
coarse blocky structure, common fine and medium distinct olive yellow (5Y 6/6) and yellowish
brown (10YR 5/8) redox concentrations; over 64 to 75 inches of dark olive gray (5Y 3/2) clay,
olive gray (5Y 4/2) dry, weak coarse angular blocky structure, common fine and medium distinct
olive yellow (2.5Y 6/6; 5Y 6/8) and few coarse distinct light olive brown (2.5Y 5/4) redox
concentrations.
GMI returned to the site from 13–18 November 2006 and excavated seven test trenches. Once
the trench profiles were investigated, GMI archeologists placed two 1-x-1-m test units off the
edge of the two most southeasterly trenches and four 50-x-50-cm units off the edge of four other
83

trenches. The average dimensions of the trenches excavated on this site were 4.6 m long, with the
upper 3 ft of the trench stepped to a width of 1.3 m and the bottom of the trench having a width of
0.7 m and averaging a depth of 1.7 m bs. The stratigraphic profiles were recorded and inspected
by the Project Archeologist. A test unit was extended off all the trenches with the exception of
Trench F, which lacked a buried A horizon. Each trench and test unit position was documented
using GPS readings. Once these trenches were profiled and the adjacent overburden stripped
from the locations where the test unit were to be located, GMI archeologists laid out two 1-x-1-m
test units and four 50-x-50-cm units with one side open to the trench profile. These units were
then excavated in 10-cm arbitrary levels with all excavated materials screened through ¼-inch
hardware cloth. These units were excavated to 10 cm below the cultural-bearing layers or until it
was obvious excavations had entered the underlying AC horizon.
In total, 179 artifacts were recovered from this site. Backhoe trench excavations yielded a piece
of FCR, one piece of charcoal, and four bones. The material from the test units consists of three
stone tools (two projectile points and a fragment of an end scraper), 20 pieces of FCR, 10 pieces
of debitage, a piece of charcoal, 67 fragments of bone, 25 pieces of shell, and 47 pieces of burned
clay/earth. The lithic assemblage from site 41COL173 contains two projectile points, one
fragment of an exhausted end scraper, eight flakes, two pieces of shatter, and 21 fragments of
FCR. All of the lithic materials are identified as being of local raw materials, with the exception
of one projectile point. The projectile points were typed as a Catahoula point (Bell 1960:16;
Justice 1987:237; Turner et al. 1999:206) and a Cuney point (Suhm and Jelks 1962:271; Turner et
al. 1999:210). The faunal assemblage from this site involved 71 specimens, with white-tailed
deer the most common mammal. Other identified species were bison, as well as one bone
fragment identified as that of a commonly found rat assumed to be an intrusion into the deposits.
The remaining 31 faunal bones were so badly broken, burned, and weathered that they were
unidentifiable. Excavations on site 41COL173 yielded a total of 47 pieces of burned clay/earth
and 25 fragments of mussel shell
Site 41COL173 is a low-density, single-component site in an environment characterized by
rapidly aggrading sediments, which have contributed to heightened levels of preservation. The
site has been buried 1–1.3 m below the surface by rapidly accumulating alluvial deposits, has
exceptional levels of preservation allowing for the recovery of fauna material, and has intact
stratigraphic sequences containing diagnostic artifacts. Thus, GMI recommended additional
archeological investigation at 41COL173 through the use of limited-sized block excavations
placed over the sections of the site with the highest densities of cultural materials.
Recommendations also included the collection of flotation samples in the form of representative
columns, with the number of columns to be determined by the areal extent of the excavation
block. Subsequently, additional eligibility testing was undertaken at site 41COL173 under a later
contract.

SITE 41DL203 (WA 17)
The development and preparation of the scope of work for testing 41DL203 was completed as a
work authorization under this contract. Fieldwork was not conducted under this contract but was
initiated in November 2007 under a separate contract.

84

SITE 41DN445 (WA 16)

Previous Investigations
Site 41DN445 is located east of the project corridor on a ridge south of Hickory Creek. The site
was initially identified in 1987 by a survey of the Lewisville Lake area by the Institute of Applied
Science at North Texas State University (now the University of North Texas) (Lebo and Brown
1990). It consisted of a prehistoric lithic surface scatter as well as a scatter of historic artifacts
and a possible root cellar feature. Due to the paucity of artifacts and poor contextual integrity
resulting from steep slopes and heavy erosion, the site was considered not eligible for inclusion in
the NRHP.
AR Consultants surveyed the originally proposed highway corridor in the winter of 1996–1997
(Skinner 1997). The only cultural material observed within the proposed corridor was a cobble of
Uvalde gravel modified into a core. In addition, two historic structures were identified outside
the corridor and both had been modified. Since site 41DN446 (west of the corridor) was located
on the same landform as 41DN445 and also consisted of both prehistoric and historic artifacts,
and because they were close enough to the project limits to be of concern, sites 41DN445 and
41DN446 were reexamined. The AR Consultants investigator agreed with the original
assessment that the artifact concentration at 41DN445 was sparse and that the site lacked
contextual integrity. Overall conclusions from the survey of the project corridor were that the
upland setting and distance from reliable, permanent water sources would have limited past
human occupation and would explain the paucity of archeological resources within the area.
Ground surface erosion was also extensive throughout the survey area, and no further
investigations were recommended.

Current Investigations
Although the Texas Historical Commission concurred with the recommendations based on
investigations by AR Consultants, changes to the original design of the corridor were later
introduced. These consisted of 0.35 acres of additional ROW to widen areas to facilitate vehicle
turns in three areas; 15 drainage easements (total 5.49 acres) for storm water runoff; and six trail
easements (total 4.02) acres. Thus, the 9.86 acres not previously surveyed were the priority for
the present investigation. On October 2–3, 2006, GMI attempted to revisit site 41DN445.
The geology of the area consists of the Upper Cretaceous Woodbine Formation, although small
portions of Holocene alluvium and Pleistocene fluviatile terrace deposits occur near Hickory
Creek (BEG 1991). The mapped soils are the Birome-Rayex-Aubrey complex, fine sandy loam,
2–15 percent slopes (Ford and Pauls 1980). The soils typically consisted of 5 to 10 cm of
yellowish brown (10YR 5/4) or strong brown (7.5YR 4/6) sandy clay loam above yellowish
brown (10YR 5/8) sandy clay. Sandstone gravels were frequently encountered in the shovel tests.
No archeological materials were present within these shovel test units, and none were observed
on the surrounding ground surfaces. The vegetation of the mapped site location consists of
grasses, briers, vines, brush, and prickly pear, as well as several areas of relatively recent growths
of oak, elm, hickory, mesquite, and juniper.

85

Based on the results of the previous investigations, observations in the new portions of the APE,
and familiarity with the natural environment of the region, the original alignment was spotchecked for surface materials. The site area was shovel tested and visually examined, particularly
along the hiking/equestrian trails that extend through these areas; however, no archeological
materials were identified. A residential subdivision now stands in the general area where a
second house was reported west of the corridor, south of a set of railroad tracks that intersect with
Village Parkway.
The thin, ancient soils within both the previously surveyed highway corridor and the added
easements and ROWs have little potential for containing buried archeological deposits, and no
archeological materials were observed on the surface or in shovel tests where the possibility for
intact soils existed. Urban development has also disturbed much of the southern one-fourth of the
project APE. Previously recorded site 41DN445 is plotted near, but outside, the project APE. No
material evidence of this site was observed during the present survey, and no further work is
recommended within the current APE.

SITE 41DN446 (WA 16)

Previous Investigations
Site 41DN446 is located west of the project corridor on a ridge south of Hickory Creek. The site
was initially identified in 1987 by a survey of the Lewisville Lake area by the Institute of Applied
Science at North Texas State University (now the University of North Texas) (Lebo and Brown
1990). It was recorded as a prehistoric lithic surface scatter as well as a scatter of historic
artifacts. Site 41DN446 is located on the same landform as, 41DN445 (east of the corridor). The
contextual integrity of 41DN466 was reported as somewhat better than 41DN455 due to thicker
soil deposits, and one arrow point was recovered. Minimal testing for NRHP eligibility was
recommended.
AR Consultants surveyed the originally proposed highway corridor in the winter of 1996–1997
(Skinner 1997). The only cultural material observed within the proposed corridor was a cobble of
Uvalde gravel modified into a core. Because sites 41DN445 and 41DN446 were close enough to
the project limits to be of concern, they were reexamined. Testing of 41DN446 by AR
Consultants failed to locate any buried archeological materials or evidence of human occupation,
nor were any artifacts identified on the ground surface within the project corridor. Overall
conclusions from the survey of the project corridor were that the upland setting and distance from
reliable, permanent water sources would have limited past human occupation and would explain
the paucity of archeological resources within the area. Ground surface erosion was also extensive
throughout the survey area, and no further investigations were recommended.

Current Investigations
Although the Texas Historical Commission concurred with the recommendations based on
investigations by AR Consultants, changes to the original design of the corridor were later
introduced. These consisted of 0.35 acres of additional ROW to widen areas to facilitate vehicle

turns in three areas; 15 drainage easements (total 5.49 acres) for storm water runoff; and six trail
easements (total 4.02) acres. Thus, the 9.86 acres not previously surveyed were the priority for
the present investigation. GMI attempted to revisit site 41DN446 on October 2–3, 2006.
The geology of the area consists of the Upper Cretaceous Woodbine Formation, although small
portions of Holocene alluvium and Pleistocene fluviatile terrace deposits occur near Hickory

sandy loam, 2–15 percent slopes (Ford and Pauls 1980). The soils in the shovel tests typically
consisted of 5 to 10 cm of yellowish brown (10YR 5/4) or strong brown (7.5YR 4/6) sandy clay
loam above yellowish brown (10YR 5/8) sandy clay. Sandstone gravels were frequently
encountered in the shovel tests. No archeological materials were present within these shovel test

site location consists of grasses, briers, vines, brush, and prickly pear, as well as several areas of
relatively recent growths of oak, elm, hickory, mesquite, and juniper.
Based on the results of the previous investigations, observations in the new portions of the APE,
and familiarity with the natural environment of the region, the original alignment was only spotchecked for surface materials. The site area was shovel tested and visually examined, particularly
along the hiking/equestrian trails that extend through these areas; however, no archeological
materials were identified. A residential subdivision now stands in the general area where a
second house was reported west of the corridor, south of a set of railroad tracks that intersect with
Village Parkway.

No material evidence of this site was observed during the present survey, and no further work
was recommended within the current APE.

SITE 41FK121 (WA 15)

Current Investigations
GMI identified and recorded site 41FK121 based on information from Mr. Gary Cheatwood, who
observed cultural materials being disturbed during construction along US 271. The site is on the
east edge of the ROW approximately 150 m northwest of the US 271 bridge over Sanders Slough.
The surface of the site area within the ROW had been cleared of vegetation. The geology of the
area consists of Holocene alluvium on a Pleistocene terrace, and the soils in the three small areas
have been mapped as Kaufman clay, frequently flooded (Roberts 1990; Thomas 1977).
Stratigraphy from units with cultural material was typified by 0–25/30 cm bd of very dark grayish
brown (10YR 3/2, dry) fill mainly of redeposited topsoil from the adjacent scraped area mixed
with mulch, very blocky and compact; over 25/30–50 cm bd of brown (10YR 5/3, dry) sandy
loam, upper part mixed with pieces of dark clay and mulch, grading to cleaner sandy loam with
cultural materials; over 50–52 cm bd of light gray (10YR 7/1, dry) pale sugar sand; over 52–135
cm bd of grayish brown (2.5Y 5/2) and reddish yellow (7.5YR 6/8, dry) mottled clay. In units
87

without cultural material, the clay typically began at the surface (0–5 cm bs) with a brown (10YR
3/2) clay fill and introduced mulch, over 4–40 cm bs of dark brown (10YR 2/2) silty clay, over
40–80 cm bs of brown (10YR 5/3) and grayish brown (2.5Y 5/2) slightly sandy clay. The
cultural deposits are limited to an area of about 12-x-5 m of sandy loam sediments; the thickness
of this remaining deposit is approximately 20–30 cm. The small portion of this site that exhibits
the intact sandy loam sediments is the area that the informant referred to as the sandy pimple
mound or “sand boil.” Areas between these “sand boils” contain continuous clay profiles with no
expression of the sandy loam matrix.
From August 14–25, 2006, GMI excavated nine shovel tests and three 1-x-1-m test units to
further characterize the nature of the archeological deposits (Figure 16). Cultural materials were
recovered from four shovel tests and all three test units, all within an area of about 12-x-5 m. No
cultural features were identified. Artifacts consisted predominantly of chipped stone and FRC:
four bifaces, 106 flakes, 12 pieces of shatter; 5.6 kilograms (kg) of FCR; and one ground stone.
The only diagnostic artifact was a possible Gary dart point from the upper 10 cm of a shovel test
in clearly redeposited materials used to create the erosion control berm. The ground stone was a
piece of quartz sandstone used as an abrader, recovered from a test unit.
Based on the possible Gary point, the site is dated to the Late Archaic period. This point,
however, was found in the upper 10 cm of a unit on the redeposited erosion control berm and is
therefore not in its original context. Although chipped stone debitage and bifaces, the possible
Gary point, an abrader, and FCR were recovered, no features or artifact concentrations were
found. Extensive disturbance within the ROW during construction severely impacted the
integrity of the upper portion of the cultural deposit, and a few artifacts appeared to have
migrated vertically through the large vertisolic cracks. Therefore, the portion of site 41FK121
investigated within the current APE revealed little or no research potential, and no further
archeological research within this area of the site was recommended.

SITE 41FK122 (WA 15)

Current Investigations
GMI identified site 41FK122 also based on information from Mr. Gary Cheatwood, who had
collected a Gary point and a piece of “obsidian” (with small white inclusions, cortex, and flake
scars) eroding from the riverbank just outside the ROW along US 271. Subsequent x-ray
fluorescence (XRF) analysis in late 2006 by Northwest Research Obsidian Studies Laboratory in
Corvallis, Oregon, and the Berkeley Archaeological XRF Laboratory, University of California,
Berkley, determined that the “obsidian” had no measurable Fe (iron), which ruled out the
possibility of its being obsidian, and was instead glass slag. According to Northwest Research
Obsidian Studies Laboratory, the “obsidian-like” qualities were likely the product of some kind of
indeterminate high-temperature industrial process, or the uncommon result of some type of hightemperature natural process (possibly forest fire or fulgurite, for example). Mr. Cheatwood
related that these materials were recovered from just above a gravel layer near the base of the
riverbank slope. This site is located within the current US 271 ROW, southeast of the Sulphur
River and southwest of the highway.

89

The area within the ROW in this area was limited due to recent disturbance by heavy machinery
and was heavily vegetated with poison ivy, sassafras, and horsetail. Recent borrow disturbance
was apparent near the base of the riverbank and in a wide swath extending west from the bridge
alignment. The riverbank here is relatively steep and the top of the slope to the flood plain is a
steep cut. The geology of the area consists of Holocene alluvium on a Pleistocene terrace and the
soils areas have been mapped as Kaufman clay, frequently flooded (Roberts 1990; Thomas 1977).
Stratigraphy within the sandy soils on top of the flood plain is typified by 0–10 cm bs of dark
brown (10YR 3/3, dry) loose silty loam organic A horizon, over 10–40 cm bs of brown (10YR
5/3, dry) relatively homogenous sandy loam, over 40–60 cm bs of dark yellowish brown (10YR
4/6) and grayish brown (2.5Y 5/2, dry) mottled orange clay.
Testing at 41FK122 involved the excavation of three controlled test units from August 14–25,
2006. A total of 271 artifacts—68 prehistoric and 203 historic—was recovered. Prehistoric
material consisted of 55 pieces of lithic debitage, one small potsherd with brushed decoration, 11
pieces of baked clay, a shell fragment, 101.6 g of bone, and 263 g of FCR, all from one test unit,
with the exception of three pieces of quartzite possible shatter. Historic-age artifacts consisted of
five various building materials, eight historic ceramic fragments, 113 pieces of glass, two small
shell buttons, and four pieces of plastic.
The site boundary probably extends beyond the ROW to the southwest onto private property.
The prehistoric period of occupation for this site is likely Late Prehistoric, based on the single
brushed sherd of prehistoric pottery.
The prehistoric artifacts could not be isolated
stratigraphically; they came from a stratum that also contained historic-age and recent materials,
with underlying strata also containing recent materials. The historic materials ranged in age from
late nineteenth century (represented by cut nails) to very recent.
Site 41FK122 is heavily disturbed and spatially limited within the ROW. One piece of
prehistoric pottery was recovered from this site; below it, however, a plastic twist tie and glass
were recovered. Due to the limited nature of this site and the degree of vertical mixing, as well as
the paucity of diagnostic artifacts and lack of features or artifact concentrations, the investigated
portion of the site within the ROW has little or no research potential. Therefore, no further
archeological work within the current APE was recommended at this site.

SITE 41HU80 (WA 21)

Current Investigations
Site 41HU80 is a newly recorded historic site located 0.5 miles east of the Collin/Hunt county
line on property owned by Jimmy Ray Saunders et al. The underlying geology of the area is the
Upper Cretaceous Marlbrook Marl (BEG 1991). The mapped soils for the area are Houston black
clay (Lane and Janak 1981). Area vegetation consists of a plowed agricultural field surrounding a
small stand of trees, mostly hackberry and native juniper.
Surficial evidence of a structure consisted of a cylindrical brick-lined well and historic cultural
material. Six shovel tests were excavated in order to recover material to establish the type of
structure and a date of occupation. The material recovered consisted of one piece of metal and
glass jewelry, three buttons (one glass, one metal, one plastic—post-1942), two machine-made
90

glass bottle necks (post-1910), two machine-made bottle caps, one small machine-made perfume
bottle (post-1910), four bottle glass fragments, one window glass fragment, two marbles (one
machine-made glass, one stoneware—1884–1918; Randall 1979), one wire nail (post-1890), one
spade connector, one three-holed brick, and one wheat penny (date obscured, though minted
between 1909 and 1958). Although material was recovered from the surface and two shovel
tests, the majority came from 20–40 cm bs in Shovel Test 4, which was in the middle of the site.
The material recovered from the site places the occupation in the early to mid-twentieth century
and is typical of a farmstead from that period. Computerized deed records in the Hunt County
Clerk’s Office are only available from the mid-1980s to the present. Research revealed that at
some point in the past Juanita Saunders acquired the property from her husband, sold it to her
children, who in turn sold it back to Juanita, and then reacquired it on her death. No information
regarding structures was found on the available deeds, and no structures are shown on the 1939
soil survey map for the county. The 1981 county soil map shows a house and barn in the vicinity,
but the location does not correspond to the site location. Site 41HU80 contains limited research
potential because of the lack of significant intact deposits, defined living surfaces, or well-defined
features, other than the brick well. Therefore, no further work was recommended for this site
within the current APE.

SITE 41MR64 (WA 14)

Previous Investigations
The Texas Archeological Sites Atlas indicates that site 41MR64, a historic town site, occurs on
the east side of SH 49 in the vicinity of the anticipated road improvements. The site dates from
the middle nineteenth century, and the investigators noted that the property abstract lists a
foundry, two houses, a Methodist church and associated cemetery, an African-American church
and cemetery, and at least one schoolhouse on the 40-acre tract surveyed. Additional structures
were indicated on a historic town map that was not obtained until after the fieldwork was
conducted. During the survey, remnants of at least two house sites and the foundry were
identified, and the two cemeteries appeared to still be in use. Observed artifacts included
fragments of whiteware, stoneware, porcelain, lavender glass, cut nails, and bricks. Some
materials demonstrated evidence of burning. The site was recommended as potentially eligible
for inclusion in the NRHP and for designation as an SAL. It is presently a designated SAL.

Current Investigations
The site occurs on gently sloping uplands dissected by creek tributaries. The portion of the site
investigated (SH 49 ROW) is in a public park on gently sloping, grass-covered terrain. Mixed
pine and hardwood trees flank the park to the west and east. Survey consisted of walkover and
surface inspection of the APE, as well as systematic shovel testing of areas that were not visibly
eroded or disturbed. The surroundings south of SH 49 consist of residential structures, yards, and
a tavern. The western one-quarter is pasture. A public park flanked by pasture to the west and
residences to the east occurs north of SH 49. The eastern one-fifth of the APE north of SH 49
consists of residential lawns that are heavily eroded. No archeological materials were observed in
these exposed areas. The remaining portions along the northeast side suggested the presence of
91

intact soil deposits and were systematically shovel tested. The vegetation consists primarily of
grass. Mixed pine and hardwood trees line the roadway along the pasture areas and the eastern
portion of the park area. Trees are present, but less densely clustered along the park and
residential areas.
The underlying geology of the area consists entirely of Eocene Queen City Sand (BEG 1975).
The mapped soils for the area are Bowie fine sandy loam, 2 to 5 percent slopes; (Natural
Resources Conservation Service n.d.; 2004). Shovel test profiles generally consisted of 5 to 10
cm of brown (7.5YR 4/4) or yellowish brown (10YR 5/4) fine sandy loam filled with ironstone
gravels, above 5 to 25 cm of light yellowish brown (10YR 6/4) gravelly fine sandy loam. Subsoil
varied from yellowish brown (10YR 5/6) sandy clay loam with strong brown (7.5YR 5/8) mottles
to yellowish red (5YR 5/8) clayey sandy loam.
Four of the 22 shovel tests excavated on the north side of the highway produced artifacts (Figure
17). Two fragments of Bristol stoneware were recovered from ST 2 at 10 cm bs. The two pieces
fit together, and a blue design is painted on exterior of the larger fragment. Delineation shovel
tests 10 m north, east, and west were sterile. A southern delineation was not excavated because it
would have been in the road ditch. No additional artifacts were observed until ST 13 near the
west end of the park. Two cut nails, six fragments of cast iron, and a piece of slag were
recovered from the upper 20 cm of this unit. Three additional cut nails and two pieces of rusted
iron were collected from the 20–40 cm bs level. ST 14 was placed 10 m east of ST 13, and
several cut nails and pieces of slag were observed. Two small fragments of rusted iron and two
small pieces of window glass were also recovered. ST 16 was excavated 10 m east of ST 14 and
did not produce artifacts, nor did ST 12, which was 20 m east of ST 16. ST 15 was placed 10 m
west of ST 13, and two pieces of iron were recovered. Several fragments of slag were also
present but not collected. Subsoil was encountered at a shallower depth than in ST 13. ST 17—
placed 5 m west of ST 15—revealed only 5 cm of topsoil; no artifacts were observed. ST 17 was
the last shovel test east of Kellyville Road. All shovel test units west of the road were sterile.
North and south delineations were not excavated because the landform descended into the road
ditch to the south and buried utility lines were present along the ROW edge, approximately 5 m
north of ST 15.
Much of the APE south of the highway did not demonstrate potential for intact soil deposits. The
western one-fourth (approximately to Kellyville Road) consists of roadbed, ditch, and deflated
soils. No culverts or driveways are present in the area, and it is unlikely that the proposed
improvements will affect this area. The APE directly south of the cluster of positive shovel tests
east of Kellyville Road descends into a residential yard, and a buried gas pipeline extends through
the area. A parking area for a tavern has disturbed the APE east of the residential property to the
Kellyville Cutoff. Two shovel tests were excavated east of the Kellyville Cutoff, but both were
sterile and indicated soil deposits less than 30 cm deep. The remainder of the east end was
heavily eroded, and a wall of ironstone boulders lines the south side of the drainage ditch to
reduce soil runoff.
Artifacts consistent with a nineteenth- to early twentieth-century iron foundry and domestic
occupation were identified in two small, discrete areas along the north side of SH 49 in the
vicinity of 41MR64. They are similar to artifacts identified within the site during a previous
investigation, and, therefore, are not likely to substantially alter the present understanding of the
site. The two artifact locales also represent very small components of a much larger site and
occur in an area that has already encountered subsurface disturbances from road construction and
92

buried utility lines. It is also probable that most of the artifacts remaining within the APE were
removed during the present investigation. Consequently, any impacts from the proposed
improvements would have no additional adverse effect on the overall contextual integrity and
research value of the site. Therefore, this small portion of site 41MR64, by itself, was not
considered eligible for inclusion in the NRHP or for designation as an SAL, and no further work
was considered necessary for this small portion of the site that falls within the current APE.

SITE 41MR99 (WA 14)

Previous Investigations
Records curated at TARL indicate that site 41MR99, a nineteenth-century iron foundry, occurs on
the east side of SH 49 in the vicinity of the anticipated road improvements. The foundry site
encompasses an area of approximately 22 acres and consists of remnants of a stone block
foundation, scatters of hand-made bricks, and several slag heaps. One iron artifact was also
identified. The site was recommended as potentially eligible for inclusion in the NRHP and for
listing as an SAL. Although the investigator recommended application for designation of the site
as an SAL in 1993, it is not presently listed.

Current Investigations
Much of the south side of SH 49 consists of a steep slope resulting from cutting into the landform
to facilitate construction of the roadway. The underlying geology of the area is Eocene Queen
City Sand (BEG 1975). The mapped soils for the area are Cuthbert gravelly fine sandy loam, 5 to
15 percent slopes; Kirvin very fine sandy loam, 2 to 5 percent slopes;, and Iulus fine sandy loam,
frequently flooded (NRCS 2004). Most shovel test units consisted of 20–30 cm of dark yellowish
brown (10YR 4/4) sandy loam with 30–50 percent ironstone gravels and cobbles above yellowish
red (5YR 5/6) fine sandy loam, also containing significant quantities of ironstone. The walls of a
tributary of nearby French Creek were also examined and the profile resembled that of the shovel
tests in the area. North of SH 49, the APE and surrounding area is generally clear-cut and
consists of grasses and brush with a sparse scatter of young pine and hardwood trees parallel to
the roadway. The area in the vicinity of the French Creek tributary consists primarily of mixed
woods and vines. Vegetation along the south side of SH 49 is also primarily wooded with young
pine and hardwood trees.
In most areas, 2–6 m of earth have been removed and it is unlikely that the proposed
improvements will affect the less disturbed portions at the top of the landform. An unpaved road
or trail also extends along the top of the landform. The south side of the roadway was walked and
visually inspected. Surface erosion was notable to the extent that ironstone was visibly eroding
through the ground surface. No artifacts or archeological features were observed.
The north side of the highway, which is the plotted location of the site, consisted of slightly more
intact soil deposits, and the area was intensively shovel tested. Initial shovel tests were placed at
30-m intervals, but spacing was reduced to 10-m intervals when what was thought to be the
western site boundary was approached. However, after six sterile shovel tests in a row that
consisted primarily of ironstone gravels and cobbles, intervals were expanded back to 30 m.
94

Although a few shovel tests extended as deep as 55 cm bs, most were no deeper than 30 cm bs.
Most shovel test units contained significant quantities of ironstone. Ironstone was also often
observed on the ground surface in many areas within and adjacent to the project APE. No
artifacts were identified in the shovel tests. Pieces of slag were occasionally observed on the
ground surface north of the ROW markers, but none was present within the ROW itself. No
artifacts, features, or paleosols were identified in the walls of the tributary of French Creek.
The south side of SH 49 has been heavily impacted by previous road construction activities, and
little intact ground surface remains within the APE. The less disturbed areas are distant enough
from the roadway that they will not be affected by the proposed improvements. Ground surface
erosion is also evident, and no archeological materials were observed in these areas. Although
pieces of slag were observed on the ground surface north of SH 49, these were all north of the
existing ROW. Shovel tests and ground inspection within the ROW indicated thin, rocky surface
soils with no archeological materials present. Although 41MR99 might have extended south of
SH 49 in the past, construction of the road appeared to have impacted that portion of the site, and
no material evidence of the site remained within the existing highway ROW. No settings with
reasonable potential to contain significant archeological deposits were noted during this
evaluation. Thus, no further work was recommended within the APE.

SITE 41MX55 (WA 6)

Previous Investigations
A member of the Texas Archeological Stewardship Network recorded the site in 1992 (Nelson
1992) on the north side of SH 11 when lithic artifacts were identified on the ground surface after
part of the ROW was trenched for installation of underground utility lines. Four artifacts,
including the distal end of a biface, were collected; however, the investigator noted that a
collector had visited the site prior to his investigation.

Current Investigations
This multicomponent site is recorded as being on the north side of SH 11, east of Boggy Creek.
The vegetation consists largely of grass although hardwood forest lines the south side of SH 11,
and clusters of young to mid-size hardwoods line the fencerow along portions of the north side.
The underlying geology of the area is Eocene Queen City Sand. Three soil units are mapped
within the project area, and these are represented in roughly equal proportions. The western
segment consists of Estes clay loam, frequently flooded. Besner-Talco complex, 0 to 2 percent
slopes, comprises the central one-third of the project area, and Bowie fine sandy loam, 2 to 5
percent slopes, accounts for the eastern one-third. Shovel test profiles north of SH 11 typically
consisted of 10–80 cm of light yellowish brown (10YR 6/4) to brownish yellow (10YR 6/6)
sandy loam above light brown (7.5YR 5/4) sandy loam. Profiles along the south side of the
highway usually indicated light yellowish brown (10YR 6/4) sandy loam.
Thirty-one shovel tests were excavated within the project area, and 14 of these produced artifacts
(Figure 18). Positive shovel tests occurred on both sides of the highway, indicating that SH 11
cuts through 41MX55. The eastern and western boundaries of the site are defined by the extent
