of positive shovel tests and intact ROW. The site likely extends across the top of the terrace
north and south of the ROW, but these areas occur on private property and could not be
investigated. Therefore, the southern and northern extents of the site are presently undefined.
The majority of the positive shovel tests contained only lithic debitage—typically one to three
small flakes. However, ST 7 yielded a quartzite cobble (16–27 cm bs) and, from depth, a grogtempered, red-slipped pottery sherd (40–60 cm bs) and a dart point (approximately 70 cm bs)
possibly consistent with the Ellis type. Two burned rock and two small pieces of charcoal were
found in ST 9. Historic artifacts—a black porcelain button (ST 15) and a piece of whiteware (ST
17)—were recovered; the button was the only material in ST 15, but a flake of heat-treated chert
was also recovered from ST 17.
Most artifacts within the project area were recovered at depths between 40 cm and 80 cm bs,
although materials were occasionally observed in higher levels. An artifact occurred in the upper
20 cm of a shovel test in only one instance (ST 14, north side). ST 7 on the south side of SH 11
indicated that archeological deposits extend deeply in some areas. The unit was situated in the
upper portion of the slope of the road cut, and, although nearly 80 cm of soil was missing relative
to the top of the present landform, artifacts were recovered for 70 additional cm.
Fourteen of the 31 shovel tests within the existing ROW contained artifacts, and positive units
extended approximately 200 m along the landform on which the site was initially identified.
Although artifact concentration was typically sparse, cultural material frequently occurred at
depths in excess of 40 cm bs, and intact buried deposits are possible. Shovel Test 7 on the south
side of SH 11 contained diagnostic artifacts, and the deposit extended approximately 160 cm
below the present ground surface.
It was uncertain how much of the existing ROW would be impacted by the proposed
improvements. If no improvements were to be planned outside existing disturbed areas, no
further investigation would be necessary. However, if any impacts (e.g., tree removal) were to be
scheduled in areas outside the already disturbed portion of the ROW, site 41MX55 could be
affected and additional investigation of these areas would be advised. In the event that grounddisturbing activities were planned beyond the limits of the existing ditches, limited testing of the
ROW for site integrity and eligibility for inclusion in the NRHP was recommended.

SITE 41MX141 (WA 4)

Previous Investigations
Site 41MX141 is located on the north side of the project area and consists of a prehistoric
habitation site. The University of Texas at Austin investigated the site in the early 1970s and
noted that the site was used for borrow for county road construction. Investigators observed lithic
debitage, bone, and shell at the site, and a local resident donated Caddoan pottery from a possible
burial at the site (Bell 1973).
In 1999, Prewitt and Associates, Inc., conducted a survey of the current project area. The flood
plain was the primary area of focus, and investigators excavated five backhoe trenches east and
west of the main creek channel. No cultural materials or features were identified in the trenches.
97

Two shovel tests were also excavated adjacent to trenches to confirm that the absence of observed
artifacts was not merely the result of poor examination of the backdirt, and no artifacts were
recovered from these units. Two additional shovel tests were excavated in the upland landform at
the east end of the project area, and again, both were sterile. As a result of the absence of
archeological materials, the investigator recommended that the construction project be allowed to
proceed (Gadus 1999). TxDOT and the Texas Historical Commission concurred with the
recommendation, and construction eventually began.

Current Investigations
In April 2006, TxDOT learned of a report that artifacts had been discovered during construction.
The project was halted and Geo-Marine was called to investigate the project area to confirm the
presence of archeological materials within the project area. Eocene silts and clays of the Reklaw
Formation underlie the upland ridges over Boggy Creek. The flood plain of the creek is
composed of Holocene alluvium (BEG 1979). The mapped soil for the site area is Lilbert loamy
fine sand (Roberts 1990). The profile of the shovel tests consisted of 15 cm to 55 cm of strong
brown (7.5YR 5/6) loamy sand with occasional hematite gravels above yellowish red (5YR 4/6)
sandy clay. The vegetation consists of small patches of grass within the ROW, although most of
it has been scraped and excavated. The property north of the ROW is grass pasture with scatters
of hardwood trees.
The entire project area—most of which had been scraped by construction equipment, often to
gray or light brown clay, or exhibited ditches that had been excavated and a foundation for a box
culvert for the relief channel that had already been constructed—was traversed and visually
inspected. Because the flood plain had been extensively trenched during the 1999 survey and no
archeological materials were identified at that time and none were observed along the exposed
surfaces during the present investigation, attention focused on a small knoll east of the relief
channel at the easternmost extent of the ROW. A single small tertiary flake was identified on the
exposed surface, and the landform was shovel tested.
The portion of the knoll within the ROW had also been scraped and an estimated 10 cm to 60 cm
of soil had been removed in many areas. Red sandy clay was visible on the surface in some
portions. A buried cable also extended along the northern ROW boundary parallel to a fence that
separates the project area from private property. The majority of the knoll occurs on the adjacent
privately owned property. Although the small tertiary flake was identified inside the ROW
during the surface inspection of this landform, no features were visible in the more exposed areas
along the knoll. Artifacts were also identified in two nonconsecutive shovel test units. A small
flake was recovered from Shovel Test 3 at a depth of 35–40 cm bs, and another small flake was
recovered from Shovel Test 5 at a depth of approximately 40–50 cm bs. Delineation shovel tests
were excavated approximately 5 m south of the positive shovel tests near the south edge of the
landform. These shovel tests tended to be shallower than the series to the north due to a heavier
degree of mechanical scraping in this area. The shovel test south of Shovel Test 5 was sterile;
however, a small chert flake was recovered from the unit south of Shovel Test 3 between 20 cm
and 30 cm bs. An additional shovel test placed 10 m west of this shovel test was sterile.
Field observations confirmed the presence of an archeological site within the ROW and the site
was reported to both TxDOT and TARL. Upon submission of a site form by GMI, TARL issued
the trinomial 41MX141 for the site. Based on the amount and degree of ground disturbance to
98

41MX141 inside the ROW and the sparseness of both surface and subsurface artifacts, it
appeared that the portion of the site within the project area had been largely destroyed by
construction activities. Any diagnostic artifacts that may have been encountered by construction
workers appeared to have been collected. No features were identified either, and this observation
suggested one or a combination of the following possibilities: (1) any features inside the project
area have been scraped away with most of the artifacts; (2) the site represents an ephemeral
occupation for which there is little material evidence; or (3) the portion of the site inside the
ROW represents a more marginal part of the site and more substantial and intact archeological
deposits may occur north of the fence line on the adjacent private property. Therefore, because of
the low density of identified artifacts, absence of archeological features, and high degree of
ground disturbance in the area, it was considered unlikely that this portion of site 41MX141
contained intact archeological materials, and no further work was recommended within the
current APE.

SITE 41SA29 (WA 7)

Previous Investigations
The Texas Archeological Sites Atlas indicates that G. E. Arnold recorded this site. No dates are
provided on the site form, although the atlas indicates that the particular form used to document
this site most commonly dates between 1939 and 1941. Site 41SA29 is plotted on the west side
of FM 3230 on a western bluff above Ayish Bayou. At the time it was recorded, the site was
roughly 1 acre in size and occurred primarily in a plowed field. Pottery and projectile points had
been reported before the site was recorded, and both types of artifacts were identified within the
road ditch at the time it was formally investigated.

Current Investigations
Site 41SA29 is plotted at the south end of the terrace on the west side of the road. The underlying
geology of the area is Eocene Reklaw formation (BEG 1993). No soil survey has been published
for San Augustine County, but red (2.5YR 5/8) sandy clay was visible on the ground surface of
the project area. The vegetation consists primarily of grass, and mixed hardwood and pine trees
line the roadway along the fence lines.
The terrace on which FM 3230 occurs had been graded to accommodate the roadway, and the
road cut extends to the fence line on the west side. In some areas, up to 50 cm of soil had been
removed. In addition, a buried cable in the ROW parallels the fence, and a trench for a utility line
was visible along the ground surface of the northern three-fourths of the project area. On the east
side, approximately 2 m of the graded landform separate the road shoulder from the eastern edge
of the ROW. At this point, the landform abruptly drops approximately 5 m into the Ayish Bayou
flood plain. Therefore, the area was visually inspected.
Upon visual examination, the area revealed the same kind of disturbances along the entire west
side of the ROW. The project area exhibited extensive grading and erosion. No indication of
41SA29 was observed in the exposed landform within the ROW, and no artifacts or features were
visible in the exposed road cut. No archeological materials were observed on the terrace
99

overlooking the site or along any other part of the project area. No settings with reasonable
potential to contain archeological deposits were noted during this evaluation, and thus no further
work was recommended within the APE.

SITE 41SA218 (WA 9)

Previous Investigations
Archeologists with Espey, Huston & Associates identified 41SA218 during a survey in 1997.
The site was recorded as a prehistoric lithic scatter plotted fewer than 30 m east of FM 2558 (also
during the same survey, site 41SA219 was recorded approximately 100 m north of this site). An
unnamed stream that empties into the Sam Rayburn Reservoir flows north of the site. Twentytwo pieces of lithic debitage, a chipped stone tool, and two pieces of worked petrified wood were
recovered from eight shovel tests in a 60-x-45-m area. The investigators considered the site to
possess limited research value due to small site size and low artifact density.

Current Investigations
The underlying geology of the area is the Eocene Yegua Formation. No soil survey has been
published for San Augustine County, nor is a soils map for the region available online through the
NRCS web soil survey page; however, two nearby shovel tests within the existing highway ROW
demonstrated 30–35 cm of brownish yellow (10YR 6/6) fine sandy loam above strong brown
(7.5YR 5/6) sandy clay loam. The project APE extends through the Angelina National Forest and
thus predominantly pine forest, mixed with deciduous trees, lines the highway; vegetation within
the ROW consists primarily of grasses. The existing ROW exhibits primarily drainage ditches on
both sides of the roadway, and most of the natural ground surface has been scraped away to
accommodate the roadbed. Ground surface erosion is particularly prominent south of CR 370,
and reddish brown subsoil was often visible along the ground surface.
The site is plotted on the east side of FM 2558 (and approximately 100 m south of 41SA219).
During the present investigation, a piece of flagging tape bearing the site trinomial, and
presumably marking the site datum, was identified. Concrete ROW markers indicate that the
ROW extends just inside the tree line in this area. Approximately 50 cm of soil has been
removed from the ROW adjacent to the 41SA218 flag to accommodate the roadbed, and the area
is eroded to the extent that subsoil and tree roots were visible. No artifacts were observed along
the exposed surface within the ROW or in exposed stream channels near the site.
The project APE exhibits extensive grading and erosion, and no archeological materials were
identified in these exposed areas. Overall, the APE possesses little potential to contain intact, in
situ archeological materials or features. The existing ROW in the vicinity of 41SA218 is heavily
disturbed with no archeological materials visible on the exposed surface. No artifacts were
visible on the ground surface or in eroded areas along the drainage north of the site either within
or outside the existing ROW. The site was previously assessed as having limited research
potential at the time of initial discovery and evaluation, and the present observations do not
contradict the original interpretations. In addition, site form and map indicate that the site was
sufficiently shovel-tested to define the site limits, and the boundary does not extend into the
100

existing ROW. No indications of site 41SA218 were identified within the project APE, and no
settings with reasonable potential to contain intact archeological deposits were noted during this
evaluation. Therefore, no further work was recommended within the current APE.

SITE 41SA219 (WA 9)

Previous Investigations
Archeologists with Espey, Huston & Associates identified 41SA219 during a survey in 1997.
The site was recorded as a prehistoric lithic scatter plotted fewer than 30 m east of FM 2558
(additionally, site 41SA218 was recorded approximately 100 m south of this site during the same
survey). An unnamed stream that empties into the Sam Rayburn Reservoir flows south of the
site. Two positive shovel tests within a 10-x-10-m area contained five pieces of debitage and a
petrified wood tool. The investigators considered the site to possess limited research value due to
small site size and low artifact density.

Current Investigations
The underlying geology of the area is the Eocene Yegua Formation. No soil survey has been
published for San Augustine County, nor is a soils map for the region available online through the
NRCS web soil survey page; however, two shovel tests within the existing highway ROW
demonstrated 30–35 cm of brownish yellow (10YR 6/6) fine sandy loam above strong brown
(7.5YR 5/6) sandy clay loam. The project APE extends through the Angelina National Forest and
thus predominantly pine forest, mixed with deciduous trees, lines the highway; vegetation within
the ROW consists primarily of grasses. The existing ROW exhibits primarily drainage ditches on
both sides of the roadway, and most of the natural ground surface has been scraped away to
accommodate the roadbed. Ground surface erosion is particularly prominent south of CR 370,
and reddish brown subsoil was often visible along the ground surface.
The site is plotted on the east side of FM 2558 (and approximately 100 m north of 41SA218).
During the present investigation, a piece of flagging tape bearing the site trinomial, and
presumably marking the site datum, was identified. Concrete ROW markers indicate that the
ROW extends just inside the tree line in this area. One shovel test was excavated adjacent to the
road ditch in the vicinity of the 41SA219 marker. The shovel test was sterile and reached subsoil
at 30 cm bs. Topsoil is much shallower than the >90-cm soil deposits mentioned on the site form,
and more than a meter of ROW has been removed to accommodate the roadway. An additional
shovel test placed 20 m to the north of the first produced similar results. A piece of unmodified
petrified wood was recovered from the unit, but it appeared to be a natural occurrence. The
ground surface south of the first shovel test is graded and eroded by the abutting stream. No
artifacts were visible on the ground surface in the vicinity, either within or adjacent to the ROW.
The project APE exhibits extensive grading and erosion, and no archeological materials were
identified in these exposed areas. Overall, the APE possesses little potential to contain intact, in
situ archeological materials or features. The two somewhat intact areas shovel-tested near
41SA219 failed to produce artifacts. No artifacts were visible on the ground surface or in eroded
areas along the drainage south of the site either within or outside the existing ROW. The site was
101

previously assessed as having limited research potential at the time of initial discovery and
evaluation, and the present observations do not contradict the original interpretations. In addition,
site form and map indicate that the site was sufficiently shovel-tested to define the site limits, and
the boundary does not extend into the existing ROW. No indications of site 41SA219 were
identified within the project APE, nor were any settings with reasonable potential to contain
intact archeological deposits noted during this evaluation. Therefore, no further work was
recommended within the APE.
SITE 41SB72 (WA 19)

Previous Investigations
The Texas Archeological Sites Atlas indicates site 41SB72 is plotted on the west side of FM 3121
and was recorded by B. Mitchum and V. Scarborough in 1977 as “a dense scatter of small chert
and quartzite flakes . . . within a tan sandy fill . . . overlying a gravel lens which contains a high
density of flakes on the surface of a 10 x 4 meter area . . . on both sides of the fence between FM
3121 and USFS land to the west . . . at the edge of the pipeline ROW.” A fragment of porcelain
was found below the surface. Investigators suggested that the surface material originated from a
buried site that had been disturbed during the installation of the adjacent pipeline. Although the
site form mentions that 41SB72 appeared partially disturbed by construction of an underground
pipeline, further investigation of the current project APE in this vicinity was undertaken to
determine if intact archeological deposits were present within the APE and at risk for adverse
impact because the form describes the site as partially occurring within the FM 3121 ROW.
Current Investigations
The site, which is within the Sabine National Forest, is approximately 1.2 miles north of Toledo
Bend Reservoir, and the area is underlain by Pleistocene fluvial terrace deposits and a narrow
ridge of Eocene Carrizo Sand. No soil survey has been published for Sabine County, and no soils
map is available for the area on the Natural Resources Conservation Service web soil survey.
However, a 1935 general soils map shows the general area consists of the Norfolk Group of
Nacogdoches soils. The only shovel test with depth greater than 20–30 cm bs produced a soil
profile of 40 cm of yellowish brown (10YR 5/4) gravelly sand above 10 cm of very compact pale
brown (10YR 6/3) sand. Vegetation immediately adjacent to the highway is regularly maintained
(mown) grasses and associated forbs, but along the edge of the APE and beyond is pine forest
interspersed with oak and sweet gum trees and shrubs.
Because the site form indicated that part of the site occurs within the FM 3121 ROW, four shovel
tests were excavated in a single transect within the existing ROW in the site vicinity to determine
if any portion of the site occurs within the proposed APE. Three shovel tests were excavated to
the north of the pipeline that was recorded on the original site form as occurring approximately 6
m south of the site. Shovel testing to the north terminated when the paved asphalt parking, which
extends northward to the shoulder of SH 21, was encountered. An additional shovel test was
placed on the south side of the pipeline easement, presumably south of the site boundary. No
artifacts were observed in any of the shovel tests, and the presence of reddish brown silty clay
subsoil at the surface of STs 2, 3, and 4 and observed over much of the area of the recorded site
location indicated extensive soil deflation. No evidence of the previously observed surface lithic
scatter or any other archeological materials were identified.
102

The original investigators hypothesized that the site might represent a buried lithic deposit that
was partially exposed when a pipeline was constructed. Although the site form mentions that the
lithic deposit appears to be thicker toward the west, which is away from the roadway and ROW,
the current shovel tests indicated either subsoil directly beneath the grass or gravelly sand above
compact sand. None of the units contained archeological materials. Disturbances associated with
the buried pipeline, a drainage ditch, and a paved asphalt parking area within the existing ROW
also limit the potential for intact, in situ archeological materials. It is also possible that the flakes
were part of the road or pipeline fill and introduced from another location. The portion of
41SB72 examined during the present investigation demonstrated a lack of contextual integrity
due to soil deflation and ground disturbances, as well as a complete absence of archeological
materials. Therefore, no further work was recommended for the section of 41SB72 that is
recorded as occurring within the limits of the present project APE.

SITE 41TT2 (WA 6)

Previous Investigations
The Texas Archeological Sites Atlas indicates that site 41TT2 is plotted near the present US 271
location and its ROW where road improvements have been proposed. When this site, as well as
site 41TT8, was originally recorded, the highway was located adjacent to the north side of the
railroad tracks, but TARL plotted the site adjacent to the present location of US 271 rather than
the original highway. Because this site was recorded in the 1930s, little information is available
through either the sites atlas or records curated at TARL. Site 41TT2 was recorded as a
prehistoric campsite located on a knoll on the south side of the south channel of Sanders Slough
and is plotted approximately 33 m east of US 271. It was excavated in 1934, and surface
collections were made during a revisit in 1971. Site records indicate that the knoll was scraped
for road fill at one time, although a small strip along a north-south fence line was left undisturbed
(Carroll 1971). In October 2004, Geo-Marine surveyed the current project area as part of a larger
survey for the TxDOT Paris District in anticipation of a realignment and widening project (Tiné
and Largent 2004). No evidence of site 41TT2 was identified within either the existing or
proposed ROW during those investigations, and no further work was recommended for this area.
TxDOT and THC concurred with the recommendations and the road expansion was allowed to
proceed.

Current Investigations
The underlying geology of the area is Pleistocene fluviatile terrace deposits. The mapped soil for
the area is Kaufman clay, frequently flooded, 0 to 1 percent slopes (Roberts 1990). The
vegetation consists of thinly to moderately clustered hardwood trees and grasses with occasional
interspersed hardwoods and conifers.
Because the project area had already been surveyed and the construction, which had been cleared
by TxDOT and THC through the 2004 survey, was underway at the time of the present
investigation, the level of effort for this current work was reduced to excavation of two shovel
tests within the ROW on the west side of the highway to confirm the absence of intact soils.
Current investigations revealed that the majority of the east side had been cut and filled to
103

accommodate new traffic lanes. Approximately the southern 100 m of ROW on the east side had
not been impacted by construction. This segment, however, was surveyed during the 2004
investigations, and no cultural materials were identified. Furthermore, the portion of ROW in this
area that is likely to be impacted by the proposed project occurs primarily within a drainage ditch
that parallels the road. Additional disturbances included a segment approximately 10 m long,
perhaps for a culvert, that had been excavated within the ROW on each side of the highway
approximately 120 m south of the south channel of Sanders Slough, and a buried cable that
extends between the road ditch and fence along the south 50 m of the west ROW. Two shovel
tests placed in the ROW west of the highway indicated little soil integrity in this area.
The project area was previously investigated in 2004 as the southern portion of a proposed
expansion of US 271 for the Paris District. During that survey, no evidence of 41TT2 was
identified within either the existing or proposed ROW, and the project area was given clearance
for construction. The current examination of the area indicated that the proposed construction is
underway, and cutting and filling activities have heavily impacted most of the ROW east of the
highway where the site was originally plotted. Therefore, inspection of the west side indicated
subsurface disturbance and a general absence of intact soil deposits. No settings with reasonable
potential to contain archeological deposits were noted during this evaluation. No further work
was recommended within the current APE.

SITE 41TT8 (WA 6)

Previous Investigations
The Texas Archeological Sites Atlas indicates that site 41TT8 is plotted near the present US 271
location and its ROW where road improvements have been proposed. When this site, as well as
site 41TT2, was originally recorded, the highway was located adjacent to the north side of the
railroad tracks, but TARL plotted the site adjacent to the present location of US 271 rather than
the original highway. Because this site was recorded in the 1930s, little information was
available through either the sites atlas or records curated at TARL. As it is mapped in the atlas,
41TT8 is a large site located on the southwest side of US 271, roughly 120 m southeast of the
channel. An old railroad grade and two structures intruded into the site according to the 1964
USGS Cuthand, TX, 7.5′ topographic quadrangle. Site 41TT8 is described as a prehistoric
campsite that contains a considerable amount of flint material and sherds. Except for a visit in
1934 when the site was presumably recorded, no known archeological investigations have
occurred.
In October 2004, Geo-Marine surveyed the current project area as part of a larger survey for the
Paris District in anticipation of a realignment and widening project (Tiné and Largent 2004). No
evidence of site 41TT8 was identified within either the existing or proposed ROW during those
investigations, and no further work was recommended for this area. TxDOT and THC concurred
with the recommendations and the road expansion was allowed to proceed.

SITE 41TT100 (WA 6)

Previous Investigations
The Texas Archeological Sites Atlas indicates that the State Department of Highways and Public
Transportation (SDHPT)—now TxDOT—recorded site 41TT100 in 1975. This site was one of
four (41TT100, 41TT101, 41TT102, and 41TT103) recorded at that time within an approximately
1.4-mile stretch of US 271.
Site 41TT100 is plotted on the east side of US 271 approximately 0.6 mile south of White Oak
Creek on a ridge extending into the flood plain. The SDHPT survey recovered several flakes and
plain pottery sherds from the surface of the site. In addition, an entire ceramic vessel was
reported found in the present roadway when US 271 was constructed in the late 1930s. No
subsurface investigations were conducted during the 1975 survey. Testing was recommended to
determine the extent of the site (Bell 1975a).
105

Current Investigations
The majority of the ridge on which site 41TT100 is located is outside of the ROW in a pasture.
The underlying geology of the area is Paleocene Midway Group undivided (BEG 1979). The
vegetation consists primarily of grasses, and mature oaks frequently line the fencerows. The
mapped soils for the area are Woodtell fine sandy loam, 2 to 5 percent slopes (Roberts 1990), and
the soils in the shovel tests generally consisted of no more than 30 cm of compact to very
compact brownish yellow (10YR 6/6) sandy loam above a strong brown (7.5YR 5/6–5/8) clay
subsoil.
Four shovel tests were placed within the ROW near this area along the east side of US 271. The
first two shovel tests were placed just below the small ridge on which the site is located, and the
remaining two were placed on the ridge. Subsoil was encountered directly below the surface in
one shovel test, and several pieces of asphalt were found in a second shovel test 30 cm below
surface, indicating a heavy disturbance. No cultural materials were observed in any shovel test.
The existing ROW demonstrates disturbance from cutting and filling activities associated with
construction of US 271, and soil erosion was frequently observed. Consequently, the project area
possesses little soil integrity and very low potential for containing archeological materials.
Therefore, no further work was recommended for site 41TT100 within the present APE.

SITE 41TT101 (WA 6)

Previous Investigations
The Texas Archeological Sites Atlas indicates that SDHPT—now TxDOT—recorded site
41TT101 in 1975. This site was one of four (41TT100, 41TT101, 41TT102, and 41TT103)
recorded at that time within an approximately 1.4-mile stretch of US 271.
Site 41TT101 was identified 0.2 miles south of 41TT100, on the first prominent hill south of the
White Oak Creek flood plain, along the east side of the ROW. SDHPT investigators observed
several flakes and pottery sherds on the ground surface, and pottery was reported when US 271
was constructed. No subsurface investigations were conducted. The site form also notes that the
main site area was probably outside of the ROW in a pasture, and the portion within the ROW
was not recommended for further work due to extensive erosion (Bell 1975b).

Current Investigations
Site 41TT101 was identified 0.2 miles south of 41TT100, on the first prominent hill south of the
White Oak Creek flood plain, along the east side of the ROW. The underlying geology of the
area is Paleocene Midway Group undivided (BEG 1979). The vegetation consists primarily of
grasses, and mature oaks frequently line the fencerows. The mapped soils for the area are
Woodtell-Raine complex, 1 to 3 percent slopes (Roberts 1990), and the soils in the shovel tests
generally consisted of no more than 30 cm of compact to very compact brownish yellow (10YR
6/6) sandy loam above a strong brown (7.5YR 5/6-5/8) clay subsoil.

106

The ROW near 41TT101 has been extensively altered, and much of the landform had been
removed to the extent that it could only accommodate one shovel test in the intact ROW. A
second shovel test was placed in the ditch to determine if any deposits had washed into this area.
Both shovel tests reached subsoil within the first 15 cm below surface and neither contained
cultural materials. The only artifact encountered was a single flake eroding from the road cut at
the base of a large oak tree. A large house and associated lawn and gardens now occupy the
landform. No cultural materials were observed in any of the shovel tests.
Only one flake was recovered at site 41TT101, and it occurred in a highly disturbed context. The
majority of the existing ROW is disturbed from previous construction activities, erosion, and
grading of the ground surface. Consequently, the project area possesses little soil integrity and
very low potential for containing archeological materials; therefore, no further work was
recommended within the present APE.

SITE 41TT102 (WA 6)

Previous Investigations
The Texas Archeological Sites Atlas indicates that SDHPT—now TxDOT—recorded site
41TT102 in 1975. This site was one of four (41TT100, 41TT101, 41TT102, and 41TT103)
recorded at that time within an approximately 1.4-mile stretch of US 271.
Site 41TT102 was recorded 0.8 miles south of 41TT101, on the first prominent hill south of an
unnamed tributary of the Johnson Branch of Ripley Creek, along the east side of the ROW.
Prehistoric artifacts were observed and investigators collected several flakes and pottery sherds.
No subsurface investigations were conducted. The investigator also noted that the main site area
was probably outside the ROW. The portion of the site within the ROW was not recommended
for further work due to disturbances from a barn as well as several drives surrounding the
structure (Bell 1975c).

Current Investigations
The underlying geology of the area is Paleocene Midway Group undivided (BEG 1979). The
vegetation consists primarily of grasses, and mature oaks frequently line the fence rows. The
mapped soils for the area are Woodtell-Raine Complex, 1 to 3 percent slopes (Roberts 1990), and
the soils in the shovel tests generally consisted of no more than 30 cm of compact to very
compact brownish yellow (10YR 6/6) sandy loam above a strong brown (7.5YR 5/6-5/8) clay
subsoil.
Four shovel tests were placed in the ROW along the east side of US 271 adjacent to the recorded
location of 41TT102. All units reached subsoil within 10 cm below surface, and the northern two
contained large pieces of concrete just above the subsoil. No archeological materials were
recovered from any of the shovel tests.

Another shovel-tested area along U.S 271 was a prominent ridge located approximately 50 m

Previous Investigations

Site 41TT103 is located, approximately 0.4 miles south of 41TT102, on a small ridge north of
Ripley Creek. SDHPT archeologists collected potsherds from the site surface, but no subsurface

1975d).

CHAPTER 6
CONCLUSIONS AND RECOMMENDATIONS

RESULTS
Twenty-nine work authorizations were issued under TxDOT Contract No. 575 xx SA010 (see
Table 2): three impact evaluations, 19 archeological surveys, two scopes of work (testing
strategies for prehistoric sites 41COL172/41COL173 and 41DL203), one testing project (sites
41COL172 and 41COL173), and one archival research project; two work authorizations were
cancelled prior to initiation of work, and the final work authorization consisted of production of
this report. The work was conducted in the TxDOT districts of Atlanta, Dallas, Lufkin, Paris, and
Tyler under THC Antiquities Permit 4068. Individual interim reports were coordinated with the
THC through TxDOT Environmental Affairs Division. Artifact collections taken from state
property and from private property with the landowner’s consent and all associated notes and
materials are curated at TARL, University of Texas at Austin.
Twenty-eight sites were investigated during this contract (see Table 4). Preliminary archival
research revealed 20 sites plotted within or adjacent to highway ROWs within proposed project
areas. Fourteen of these sites (41AG180, 41DN445, 41DN446, 41MR99, 41SA29, 41SA218,
41SA219, 41SB72, 41TT2, 41TT8, 41TT100, 41TT101, 41TT102, and 41TT103) were not found
because they had been completely destroyed within the ROW, did not extend into the ROW, or
were misplotted on TARL maps.
Fourteen sites were revisited or newly recorded. Six previously recorded sites (41COL172,
41COL173, 41DL203, 41MR64, 41MX55, and 41MX141) were found (41DL203 has been
previously recorded but not yet investigated; one work authorization under this current contract
addressed the testing scope of work for this site), and eight sites (41AG194, 41AN162, 41AN173,
41CE404, 41CE405, 41FK121, 41FK122, and 41HU80) were newly identified and recorded. Of
these 14 sites (or portions of sites) that were identified within areas of potential effects, seven
(41AN173, 41CE404, 41COL172, 41COL173, 41DL203, 41FK121, and 41MX141) contained
only prehistoric artifacts; three sites (41CE405, 41HU80, and 41MR64) contained only historicage artifacts; and four sites (41AG194, 41AN162, 41FK122, and 41MX55) contained both
prehistoric and historic material.

109

Ten of the 14 sites (41AN162, 41AN173, 41CE404, 41COL172, 41COL173, 41DL203,
41FK121, 41FK122, 41MR64, and 41MX55) exhibit only subsurface cultural material; four
(41AG194, 41CE405, 41HU80, and 41MX141) produced primarily subsurface material, but at
least one artifact was found on the surface of each. At sites 41CE405 and 41HU80, historic-age
structural remains were present as well.
Although previous mechanical trenching identified sites 41COL172 and 41COL173 (Sills et al.
2005), current trenching efforts identified no sites. Typically, fewer mechanical trenches per acre
than shovel tests per acre were excavated. The negative assessment of these trenched project
areas for intact cultural deposits, however, is most likely accurate due to the large vertical and
horizontal exposure of sediments achieved by applying this excavation method.
Of the 28 sites investigated under this contract, further testing was recommended for seven sites
within the proposed areas of potential effects: new sites 41AN173 (prehistoric), and 41AG194
and 41AN162 (both multicomponent); and previously recorded sites 41MX55 (multicomponent),
sites 41COL172 and 41COL173 (prehistoric, scope of work developed and sites tested under this
contract), and 41DL203 (prehistoric, scope of work developed under this contract; testing under a
separate contract). Further archival research only was suggested for site 41CE405. There was no
evidence of 14 of the previously recorded sites within the APEs (see Table 4). No further
archeological investigations were considered warranted for the remaining seven sites within the
investigated proposed project APEs.
EVALUATION AND DISCUSSION OF STRATEGIES
In most cases, impact evaluations are conducted prior to a full archeological survey involving
shovel testing, mechanical trenching, or a combination of both. If observations made during the
evaluation effort reveal extensive natural and man-made disturbances that preclude the presence
of archeological historic properties or state archeological landmarks and thus obviate the
necessity for an intensive survey, the impact evaluation is often the only archeological field
assessment that many project areas undergo. Conversely, when survey is warranted, gathering
information based on impact evaluations expedites intensive survey planning through project
area-specific information, even when large tracts of new ROW necessitate mechanical trenching.
The location of utilities, the number and placement of trenches, soil and geological contexts, and
access restrictions—all information germane to the survey—can be predetermined with an impact
evaluation. For impact evaluations to be effective, however, researchers must have specific
project descriptions, scaled drawings, and right-of-entry to any new ROW, which, unfortunately,
is not the case in some project areas. However, for proposed project areas where access has been
denied, the impact evaluation can still be a valuable tool in determining whether or not an
intensive survey is warranted for that property through observations taken from adjacent areas.
An archeological survey usually follows an impact evaluation. Information from the impact
evaluation often results in intensive archeological survey of only short segments of a proposed
highway or road project where there is potential for, or existence of, archeological deposits or
sites rather than an intensive survey of the entire project APE. As with impact evaluations,
specific project descriptions and scaled drawings as well as right-of-entry are necessary,
particularly when conducting investigations in unassessed areas of proposed APEs outside of
preexisting ROWs. All shovel testing and trenching projects are conducted within the current
Council of Texas Archeologists guidelines as approved by THC, and archeological sites are
normally found at this level of investigation. Sites can be assessed and recommendations made
for either no further work or for testing to determine NRHP eligibility and/or SAL designation.
110

The survey efforts summarized in this report reinforce two aspects of archeological properties
within existing highway ROWs: (1) the previous disturbance related to road construction, utility
trenches, and maintenance often destroys the contextual integrity of site contexts; and (2) the
plotting of sites recorded long ago is often inaccurate. If one removes the previously recorded
sites from the APEs that were surveyed, the survey of 11 geographically distinct areas yielded a
total of eight new sites (41AG194, 41AN162, 41AN173, 41CE404, 41CE405, 41FK121,
41FK122, and 41HU80). Of these eight sites, sites 41AG194, 41AN162, 41AN173 (scraping for
subsurface features), and 41CE405 (archival research only) were deemed worthy of additional
investigations. The remaining sites lacked sufficient contextual integrity to warrant additional
investigations. Of the 17 previously recorded sites located along existing ROWs, no evidence of
14 of the sites (see Table 4) was found within the APE. The remaining three sites were
considered to have limited research potential.

RECOMMENDATIONS
Several recommendations can be made to promote a more consistent return of the necessary
information from the field. Some recommendations reiterate similar recommendations offered in
previous summary reports (e.g., Largent et al. 2005; Peukert et al. 2003; Phillips et al. 2007; and
Wurtz et al. 2003), but they bear repeating:
1. Clear rights-of-entry should be obtained in advance for all privately owned portions of the
proposed project areas. In many cases, right-of-entry has not been obtained at the time of
evaluation or survey, limiting the effectiveness of the investigations and making it
impossible to identify new sites or reexamine previously recorded sites and structures.
2. Gathering available map and archival information prior to fieldwork and a subsequent
recommendation is essential for a cost-effective approach. Many of the work authorizations
under this contract involved an APE with previously recorded sites that needed to be
revisited to determine their existence/condition/eligibility status. Thus, literature and sites
atlas research related to site locations is imperative for gathering known data and identifying
plotted locations of previously known sites. Five new historic-era sites or site components
were recorded during the current contract. To facilitate location and identification of
potential historic-era sites and structures within the ROW or APE during fieldwork, review
of The Texas Historic Overlay (Foster et al. 2006) and a review of both current and early
editions of topographic, soils, and county road maps prior to undertaking an impact
evaluation or archeological survey remains essential. In instances where historic-era cultural
remains are encountered, additional resources/archives may also warrant careful
examination.
3. Impact evaluations remain an essential tool for identifying areas for survey, trenching, or
both, as well as for clearing specific areas for additional construction projects. Only three
work authorizations under this contract were issued for impact evaluations. These
evaluations were undertaken to identify any remaining relevant areas for survey or trenching
(e.g., undeveloped, undisturbed areas) and to clear previously surveyed areas for specific,
relatively low-impact projects (e.g., safety end treatments for bridges).

111

4. Survey remains essential in locating sites within project areas. The majority of work
authorizations under the current contract were for survey. Many of the investigations
focused specifically on revisiting plotted locations of previously known sites to determine
their existence and status, although eight new sites were identified through survey in
primarily newly acquired property in unassessed areas. Nevertheless, information derived
from examination of the 102 archeological sites known to have been recorded in the 437
project areas examined under contracts 570 xx PD002 (Wurtz et al. 2003), 570 xx PD003
(Peukert et al. 2003), 572 xx SA012 (Largent et al. 2005), 574 xx SA004 (Phillips et al.
2007), and this current contract 575 xx SA010 indicates that sites are rare, occurring in
approximately 23 percent of the project areas examined. This relatively low site
representation is due to the limited impact area of many of the proposed projects; previous
extensive disturbance of the existing ROWs; and the presence of the project areas on loworder streams.
5. Even though mechanical trenching may have been over-recommended in previous
investigations, trenching remains essential in settings likely to retain deeply buried, intact
cultural deposits. Two sites (41COL172 and 41COL173) were discovered only through
mechanical trenching during a previous survey by Sills et al. (2005). Both sites are deeply
buried in flood plain settings of Holocene alluvium. Testing during this contract revealed
intact, deeply buried features, living surfaces, copious amounts of highly preserved faunal
remains, and datable botanical material. The failure of trenching to identify cultural sites is
most likely due to the inaccurate assessment of alluvial deposits for the potential presence of
archeological deposits or paleosols. High-energy depositional environments (sands and
cobbles) and very low-energy depositional environments (back swamp/slack water) are
unlikely to contain intact archeological deposits and should be evaluated as such.
Evaluation could be partly achieved by close inspection of stream cutbanks. Then,
mechanical trenching can be judiciously focused in areas that exhibit environments with the
potential to retain deeply buried deposits. Determining the need for mechanical trenching to
assess the potential for buried archeological deposits is dependent upon the following:
♦ Experience

of the project archeologist within the region, especially knowledge of
geologic and pedologic processes. The forces of wind, water, and gravity throughout
the Holocene period have moved the sandy soils of East Texas. The mechanical
trenching conducted under this contract revealed that flood plain environments in East
Texas are frequently very dynamic with little potential for the preservation of
archeological contexts. Toe slopes adjacent to valley walls and the slopes of flood plain
rises are probably the primary areas that should be of concern within the East Texas
drainages. Too frequently, archeologists merely assume that a fluvial environment is
conducive to site preservation and that all fluvial deposits must be examined for buried
archeological contexts.
of prior impacts. Conducting these investigations over the length of the
contract clearly revealed that highway ROWs can be disturbed in a number of ways and
that new modifications will not necessarily affect previously undisturbed deposits.

♦ Recognition

In summary, because of Geo-Marine’s experience in undertaking these kinds of projects for
TxDOT, the results of the mechanical trenching indicate that recommendations for trenching
are often too conservative. Admittedly, one is dealing with probabilities, but the proper
balance of experience, knowledge of depositional environments, and a realistic evaluation of
existing and potential impacts should result in an efficient evaluation that will not jeopardize
historic properties.

Current

APPENDIX A
GLOSSARY OF TECHNICAL TERMS

Adze: Tool whose cutting edge is at a right angle to the handle and that is used in working wood.
Area of potential effects (APE): “Area of potential effects means the geographic area or areas
within which an undertaking may directly or indirectly cause changes in the character or use
of historic properties, if any such properties exist. The area of potential effects is influenced
by the scale and nature of an undertaking and may be different for different kinds of effects
caused by the undertaking” [36 CFR Part 800.16(d)]. Essentially, the area of potential effects
(APE) is the area of reasonably anticipated project impacts and is actually three-dimensional,
consisting of length, width, and depth.
Arrow point: Point used to tip an arrow, which is propelled by a bow.
Biface: Piece of conchoidally fracturing stone that has had flakes removed from both faces to
create a tool.
Burin: Chisel-like tool presumed to have been used in working bone, antler, and wood.
Dart point: Point used to tip a throwing spear or dart, which is propelled by an atlatl.
Debitage: Debris generated by the removal through percussion/pressure of flakes, chips, and
chunks to make stone tools.
Fill section: Introduced fill used to elevate the approaches to a bridge above the surrounding
terrain.
Flake: Generally thin piece of conchoidally fractured stone with a positive bulb of percussion
showing that it was removed from the parent piece by percussion/pressure.
Gouge: Generally thick, bifacially modified tool presumed to have been used like an adze.
Grog: Crushed fired clay added as temper to clay used in making ceramic vessels.
Historic property: “Historic property means any prehistoric or historic district, site, building,
structure, or object included in, or eligible for inclusion in, the National Register of Historic
Places maintained by the Secretary of the Interior. This term includes artifacts, records, and
remains that are related to and located within such properties. The term includes properties
of traditional religious and cultural importance to an Indian tribe or Native Hawaiian
organization and that meet the National Register criteria. The term eligible for inclusion in
the National Register includes both properties formally determined as such in accordance
with regulations of the Secretary of the Interior and all other properties that meet the National
Register criteria” [36 CFR 800.16(l)]. Essentially, a historic property is a cultural resource
included in, or eligible for inclusion in, the National Register of Historic Places.
Impact Evaluation: Onsite inspection documenting existing impacts or other conditions that
may preclude the presence of intact archeological deposits within the project area for a
proposed Transportation Activity.
Mano: Handheld stone used, usually with a metate, to grind plant parts such as seeds.

A-3

Megafauna: Particularly large animal such as bison and mammoth.
Metate: Anvil of stone used, usually with a mano, to grind plant parts such as seeds.
Midden: Accumulation of occupational debris, particularly organic remains.
Projectile point: Inclusive term for arrow and dart points.
Scraper: Tool with generally thick, unifacially modified edges used to work hides, bone, and
wood.
Sherd: A piece of broken pottery.
State Archeological Landmark (SAL): The Texas Historical Commission “uses one or more of
the following criteria when assessing the appropriateness of official landmark designation,
and/or the need for further investigations under the permit process:
(1) the site has the potential to contribute to a better understanding of the prehistory and/or
history of Texas by the addition of new and important information;
(2) the site’s archeological deposits and the artifacts within the site are preserved and
intact, thereby supporting the research potential or preservation interests of the site;
(3) the site possesses unique or rare attributes concerning Texas prehistory and/or history;
(4) the study of the site offers the opportunity to test theories and methods of preservation,
thereby contributing to new scientific knowledge;
(5) the high likelihood that vandalism and relic collecting has occurred or could occur, and
official landmark designation is needed to insure [sic] maximum legal protection, or
alternatively further investigations are needed to mitigate the effects of vandalism and
relic collecting when the site cannot be protected” [Texas Administrative Code (13
TAC Part 2.§26.8)]. Essentially, a state archeological landmark (SAL) is the state
equivalent of a historic property.
Survey: Fieldwork to locate archeological remains within the project area for a proposed
Transportation Activity, including on-foot examination of the surface, shovel testing, and
trenching by mechanical means where appropriate.
Survey with Geoarcheological Evaluation: Fieldwork to locate archeological remains within
the project area for a proposed Transportation Activity, including examination and recording
of trench walls or other exposures by a geomorphologist, quaternary geologist, physical
geographer, soil scientist, or archeologist with the formal training and experience to apply the
principles of geology to the evaluation of the pedological, stratigraphic, geomorphic,
anthrogenic, and other conditions affecting the physical integrity of archeological deposits
and the interpretation of archeological materials.
Temper: Nonplastic materials added to clay to decrease the risk of cracking when firing ceramic
vessels.
Transportation Activity: Any proposed project involving the development, design, construction,
or maintenance of the state’s intermodal transportation system.
